**Graphene** ()[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-camdic-1) is a variety of the [element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_element "Chemical element")[carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon") which occurs naturally in small amounts. In graphene, the carbon forms a sheet of interlocked [atoms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atom "Atom") as hexagons one carbon atom thick. The result resembles the face of a [honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honeycomb "Honeycomb"). When many hundreds of graphene layers build up, they are called graphite.

Commonly known types of carbon are [diamond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamond "Diamond") and [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite"). In 1947, Canadian physicist P. R. Wallace suggested carbon would also exist in sheets. German chemist Hanns-Peter Boehm and coworkers isolated single sheets from graphite, giving them the name graphene in 1986. In 2004, the material was characterized by Andre Geim and Konstantin Novoselov at the University of Manchester, England. They received the 2010 Nobel Prize in Physics for their experiments.

In technical terms, graphene is a [carbon allotrope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_allotrope "Carbon allotrope") consisting of a [single layer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single-layer_materials "Single-layer materials") of atoms arranged in a [honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_lattice "Hexagonal lattice") planar [nanostructure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanostructure "Nanostructure").[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-peres2009-3) The name "graphene" is derived from "[graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite")" and the suffix [-ene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/-ene "-ene"), indicating the presence of double bonds within the carbon structure.

Graphene is known for its exceptionally high [tensile strength](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultimate_tensile_strength "Ultimate tensile strength"), [electrical conductivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity "Electrical resistivity and conductivity"), [transparency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency "Transparency and translucency"), and being the thinnest two-dimensional material in the world.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-4) Despite the nearly transparent nature of a single graphene sheet, graphite (formed from stacked layers of graphene) appears black because it absorbs all visible light wavelengths.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nair2008-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhu2014-6) On a microscopic scale, graphene is the strongest material ever measured.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lee2008-7)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-cao2020-8)

[![Image 2](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Graphene_visible.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_visible.jpg)

Photograph of a suspended graphene membrane in transmitted light. This one-atom-thick material can be seen with the naked eye because it absorbs approximately 2.3% of light.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nair2008-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhu2014-6)

The existence of graphene was first theorized in 1947 by [Philip R. Wallace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P._R._Wallace "P. R. Wallace") during his research on graphite's electronic properties, while the term _graphene_ was first defined by [Hanns-Peter Boehm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanns-Peter_Boehm "Hanns-Peter Boehm") in 1987.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-9) In 2004, the material was isolated and characterized by [Andre Geim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andre_Geim "Andre Geim") and [Konstantin Novoselov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Novoselov "Konstantin Novoselov") at the [University of Manchester](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Manchester "University of Manchester")[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2004-10)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-aps2009-11) using a piece of graphite and [adhesive tape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pressure-sensitive_tape "Pressure-sensitive tape").[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-12) In 2010, Geim and Novoselov were awarded the [Nobel Prize in Physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics "Nobel Prize in Physics") for their "groundbreaking experiments regarding the two-dimensional material graphene".[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-13) While small amounts of graphene are easy to produce using the method by which it was originally isolated, attempts to scale and automate the manufacturing process for mass production have had limited success due to cost-effectiveness and quality control concerns.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-14)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-15) The global graphene market was $9 million in 2012,[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-azon2014-16) with most of the demand from research and development in [semiconductors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiconductor_device "Semiconductor device"), electronics, [electric batteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_battery "Electric battery"),[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-mrmak2014-17) and [composites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composite_material "Composite material").

The [IUPAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry "International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry") (International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry) advises using the term "graphite" for the three-dimensional material and reserving "graphene" for discussions about the properties or reactions of single-atom layers.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-IUPAC2009-18) A narrower definition, of "isolated or free-standing graphene", requires that the layer be sufficiently isolated from its environment,[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2009a-19) but would include layers suspended or transferred to [silicon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon_dioxide "Silicon dioxide") or [silicon carbide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon_carbide "Silicon carbide").[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ried2009-20)

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.png/250px-Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.png)

A lump of [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite"), a graphene [transistor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transistor "Transistor"), and a [tape dispenser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tape_dispenser "Tape dispenser"). Donated to the [Nobel Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Museum "Nobel Museum") in Stockholm by [Andre Geim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andre_Geim "Andre Geim") and [Konstantin Novoselov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Novoselov "Konstantin Novoselov") in 2010.

### Structure of graphite and its intercalation compounds

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Structure of graphite and its intercalation compounds")]

In 1859, [Benjamin Brodie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sir_Benjamin_Collins_Brodie,_2nd_Baronet "Sir Benjamin Collins Brodie, 2nd Baronet") noted the highly [lamellar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamella_(materials) "Lamella (materials)") structure of thermally reduced [graphite oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite_oxide "Graphite oxide").[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2012-21)[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-brod1859-22) Pioneers in [X-ray crystallography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray_crystallography "X-ray crystallography") attempted to determine the structure of graphite. The lack of large [single crystal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single_crystal "Single crystal") graphite specimens contributed to the independent development of [X-ray powder diffraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Powder_diffraction "Powder diffraction") by [Peter Debye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Debye "Peter Debye") and [Paul Scherrer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Scherrer "Paul Scherrer") in 1915, and [Albert Hull](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Hull "Albert Hull") in 1916.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-deb1916-23)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-deb1917-24)[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-hull1917-25) However, neither of their proposed structures was correct. In 1918, Volkmar Kohlschütter and P. Haenni described the properties of [graphite oxide paper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_oxide_paper "Graphene oxide paper").[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kohl1918-26) The structure of graphite was successfully determined from single-crystal X-ray diffraction by [J. D. Bernal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Bernal "John Bernal") in 1924,[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-bern1924-27) although subsequent research has made small modifications to the [unit cell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_cell "Unit cell") parameters.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-tru1975-28)[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-howe2003-29)

The theory of graphene was first explored by [P. R. Wallace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P._R._Wallace "P. R. Wallace") in 1947 as a starting point for understanding the electronic properties of 3D graphite. The emergent massless [Dirac equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_equation "Dirac equation") was separately pointed out in 1984 by [Gordon Walter Semenoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gordon_Walter_Semenoff "Gordon Walter Semenoff"),[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Semenoff-30) and by David P. Vincenzo and Eugene J. Mele.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-divi1984-31) Semenoff emphasized the occurrence in a magnetic field of an electronic [Landau level](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landau_level "Landau level") precisely at the [Dirac point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_point "Dirac point"). This level is responsible for the anomalous integer [Quantum Hall effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_Hall_effect "Quantum Hall effect").[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2005-32)[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-gusy2005-33)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhang2005-34)

### Observations of thin graphite layers and related structures

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Observations of thin graphite layers and related structures")]

[Transmission electron microscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy "Transmission electron microscopy") (TEM) images of thin [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite") samples consisting of a few graphene layers were published by G. Ruess and F. Vogt in 1948.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ruess1948-35) Eventually, single layers were also observed directly.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-meyer2007-36) Single layers of graphite were also observed by [transmission electron microscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy "Transmission electron microscopy") within bulk materials, particularly inside soot obtained by chemical [exfoliation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exfoliation_(chemistry) "Exfoliation (chemistry)").[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-harris2018-37)

From 1961 to 1962, [Hanns-Peter Boehm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanns-Peter_Boehm "Hanns-Peter Boehm") published a study of extremely thin flakes of graphite.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-boehm1962b-38) The study measured flakes as small as ~0.4 [nm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanometre "Nanometre"), which is around 3 atomic layers of amorphous carbon. This was the best possible resolution for TEMs in the 1960s. However, it is impossible to distinguish between suspended monolayer and multilayer graphene by their TEM contrasts, and the only known method is to analyze the relative intensities of various diffraction spots.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-meyer2007-36) The first reliable TEM observations of monolayers are likely given in references 24 and 26 of Geim and Novoselov's 2007 review.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)

In 1975, van Bommel et al. [epitaxially](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epitaxy "Epitaxy") grew a single layer of graphite on top of silicon carbide.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Bom75-39) Others grew single layers of carbon atoms on other materials.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-oshi1997-40)[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-forb1998-41) This "epitaxial graphene" consists of a single-atom-thick hexagonal lattice of sp 2-bonded carbon atoms, as in free-standing graphene. However, there is significant charge transfer between the two materials and, in some cases, hybridization between the [d-orbitals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D-orbital "D-orbital") of the substrate atoms and π orbitals of graphene, which significantly alter the electronic structure compared to that of free-standing graphene.

Boehm et al. coined the term "graphene" for the hypothetical single-layer structure in 1986.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-42) The term was used again in 1987 to describe single sheets of graphite as a constituent of [graphite intercalation compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite_intercalation_compound "Graphite intercalation compound"),[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-mour1987-43) which can be seen as crystalline salts of the intercalant and graphene. It was also used in the descriptions of [carbon nanotubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_nanotube "Carbon nanotube") by R. Saito and [Mildred](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mildred_Dresselhaus "Mildred Dresselhaus") and [Gene Dresselhaus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_Dresselhaus "Gene Dresselhaus") in 1992,[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-saito1992-44) and in the description of [polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon "Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon") in 2000 by S. Wang and others.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-wang2000-45)

Efforts to make thin films of graphite by mechanical exfoliation started in 1990.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2008-46) Initial attempts employed exfoliation techniques similar to the drawing method. Multilayer samples down to 10 nm in thickness were obtained.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)

In 2002, Robert B. Rutherford and Richard L. Dudman filed for a patent in the US on a method to produce graphene by repeatedly peeling off layers from a graphite flake adhered to a substrate, achieving a graphite thickness of 0.00001 [inches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inch "Inch") (0.00025 [millimetres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millimetre "Millimetre")). The key to success was the ability to quickly and efficiently identify graphene flakes on the substrate using optical microscopy, which provided a small but visible contrast between the graphene and the substrate.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ruth2002-47)

Another U.S. patent was filed in the same year by Bor Z. Jang and Wen C. Huang for a method to produce graphene-based on exfoliation followed by attrition.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-jang2002-48)

In 2014, inventor [Larry Fullerton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larry_W._Fullerton "Larry W. Fullerton") patented a process for producing single-layer graphene sheets by graphene's strong [diamagnetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamagnetism "Diamagnetism") properties.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-49)

### Full isolation and characterization

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Full isolation and characterization")]

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg/250px-Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg)

Andre Geim and Konstantin Novoselov at the Nobel Laureate press conference, [Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences "Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences"), 2010.

Graphene was properly isolated and characterized in 2004 by [Andre Geim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andre_Geim "Andre Geim") and [Konstantin Novoselov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Novoselov "Konstantin Novoselov") at the [University of Manchester](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Manchester "University of Manchester").[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2004-10)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-aps2009-11) They pulled graphene layers from graphite with a common [adhesive tape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adhesive_tape "Adhesive tape") in a process called micro-mechanical cleavage, colloquially referred to as the Scotch tape technique.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-manch2014-50) The graphene flakes were then transferred onto a thin [silicon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon_dioxide "Silicon dioxide") layer on a [silicon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon "Silicon") plate ("wafer"). The silica electrically isolated the graphene and weakly interacted with it, providing nearly charge-neutral graphene layers. The silicon beneath the SiO

2 could be used as a "back gate" electrode to vary the charge density in the graphene over a wide range.

This work resulted in the two winning the Nobel Prize in Physics in 2010 for their groundbreaking experiments with graphene.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-manch2014-50)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ukiop2010-51)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nobel2013-52) Their publication and the surprisingly easy preparation method that they described, sparked a "graphene gold rush". Research expanded and split off into many different subfields, exploring different exceptional properties of the material—quantum mechanical, electrical, chemical, mechanical, optical, magnetic, etc.

### Exploring commercial applications

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Exploring commercial applications")]

Since the early 2000s, several companies and research laboratories have been working to develop commercial applications of graphene. In 2014, a [National Graphene Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Graphene_Institute "National Graphene Institute") was established with that purpose at the University of Manchester, with a £60 million initial funding.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-manch2014f-53) In [North East England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_East_England "North East England") two commercial manufacturers, Applied Graphene Materials[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-burn2014-54) and [Thomas Swan Limited](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Swan_(company) "Thomas Swan (company)")[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-gibs2014-55)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-thej2014-56) have begun manufacturing. [Cambridge Nanosystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambridge_Nanosystems "Cambridge Nanosystems")[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-canew2015-57) is a large-scale graphene powder production facility in [East Anglia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Anglia "East Anglia").

Graphene is a single layer of carbon atoms tightly bound in a hexagonal honeycomb lattice. It is an allotrope of carbon in the form of a plane of sp 2-bonded atoms with a molecular bond length ![Image 5: {\displaystyle R}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b0bfb3769bf24d80e15374dc37b0441e2616e33) = 0.142[nm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanometre "Nanometre") (1.42[Å](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m "Ångström")).

The area of a [hexagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon") of side ![Image 6: {\displaystyle R}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b0bfb3769bf24d80e15374dc37b0441e2616e33) being ![Image 7: {\displaystyle {\frac {3{\sqrt {3}}}{2}}R^{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e4e5d83954c7a1aa794bb5e6f04bc6de19082d66), one hexagonal unit of graphene has an area of ![Image 8: {\displaystyle {\frac {3{\sqrt {3}}}{2}}0.142^{2}=0.0524}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/916fabd64d365a2bfec3a9d9b6b641b951ef1fcf) nm 2. There are two carbon atoms per unit, together having a mass of ![Image 9: {\displaystyle 2{\frac {12}{N_{0}}}=.0399\times 10^{-18}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5d8dba865597f216f7e325258a2992e625527771) mg. The (two-dimensional) density of graphene is therefore ![Image 10: {\displaystyle 0.0399/0.0542=0.762}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c56da37261d933ebad51c50cccf9de2d6695b2c1) mg per square meter. A kilogram of graphene therefore has an area of ![Image 11: {\displaystyle 10^{6}/0.762=1.312\times 10^{6}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec9ae6edf53629638ad306b9a81365f88da09a9f) m 2 or 131.2 [hectares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hectares "Hectares").

In a graphene sheet, each atom is connected to its three nearest carbon neighbors by [σ-bonds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%A3-bond "Σ-bond"), and a delocalized [π-bond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%A0-bond "Π-bond"), which contributes to a [valence band](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valence_band "Valence band") that extends over the whole sheet. This type of [bonding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bonding_in_solids "Bonding in solids") is also seen in [polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon "Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon").[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zdet2015-58)[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-harris2018-37) The valence band is touched by a [conduction band](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conduction_band "Conduction band"), making graphene a [semimetal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semimetal "Semimetal") with unusual [electronic properties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_properties_of_graphene "Electronic properties of graphene") that are best described by theories for massless relativistic particles.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2) Charge carriers in graphene show linear, rather than quadratic, dependence of energy on momentum, and field-effect transistors with graphene can be made that show bipolar conduction. Charge transport is [ballistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ballistic_conduction "Ballistic conduction") over long distances; the material exhibits large [quantum oscillations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_oscillations "Quantum oscillations") and large nonlinear [diamagnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamagnetism "Diamagnetism").[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lizhi2015-59)

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carbon_hybrid_orbitals_-_from_s%2Bpx%2Cpy%2Cpz_to_sp%C2%B2%2Bpz.svg/250px-Carbon_hybrid_orbitals_-_from_s%2Bpx%2Cpy%2Cpz_to_sp%C2%B2%2Bpz.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carbon_hybrid_orbitals_-_from_s%2Bpx,py,pz_to_sp%C2%B2%2Bpz.svg)

Carbon orbitals 2s, 2p x, 2p y form the hybrid orbital sp 2 with three major lobes at 120°. The remaining orbital, p z, extends out of the graphene's plane.

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Graphene_-_sigma_and_pi_bonds.svg/250px-Graphene_-_sigma_and_pi_bonds.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_-_sigma_and_pi_bonds.svg)

Sigma and pi bonds in graphene. Sigma bonds result from an overlap of sp 2 hybrid orbitals, whereas pi bonds emerge from tunneling between the protruding p z orbitals.

Three of the four outer-[shell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_shell "Electron shell")[electrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron "Electron") of each atom in a graphene sheet occupy three sp 2[hybrid orbitals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_hybridization "Orbital hybridization") – a combination of orbitals s, p x and p y — that are shared with the three nearest atoms, forming σ-bonds. The length of these [bonds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93carbon_bond "Carbon–carbon bond") is about 0.142 nanometers.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-coop2012-60)[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-felix2013-61)

The remaining outer-shell electron occupies a p z orbital that is oriented perpendicularly to the plane. These orbitals hybridize together to form two half-filled bands of free-moving electrons, π, and π∗, which are responsible for most of graphene's notable electronic properties.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-coop2012-60) Recent quantitative estimates of aromatic stabilization and limiting size derived from the enthalpies of [hydrogenation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogenation "Hydrogenation") (ΔH hydro) agree well with the literature reports.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-dixit2019-62)

Graphene sheets stack to form graphite with an interplanar spacing of 0.335[nm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanometre "Nanometre") (3.35[Å](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m "Ångström")).[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-63)

Graphene sheets in solid form usually show evidence in diffraction for graphite's (002) layering. This is true of some single-walled nanostructures.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kasu2002-64) However, unlayered graphene displaying only (hk0) rings have been observed in the core of [presolar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presolar_grains "Presolar grains") graphite onions.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-bern1996-65) TEM studies show faceting at defects in flat graphene sheets[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-fraun2002-66) and suggest a role for two-dimensional crystallization from a melt.

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Graphene_SPM.jpg/250px-Graphene_SPM.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_SPM.jpg)

[Scanning probe microscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scanning_probe_microscopy "Scanning probe microscopy") image of graphene

The [hexagonal lattice structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_lattice "Hexagonal lattice") of isolated, single-layer graphene can be directly seen with transmission electron microscopy (TEM) of sheets of graphene suspended between bars of a metallic grid.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-meyer2007-36) Some of these images showed a "rippling" of the flat sheet, with an amplitude of about one nanometer. These ripples may be intrinsic to the material as a result of the instability of two-dimensional crystals,[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-carl2007-67)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-faso2007-68) or may originate from the ubiquitous dirt seen in all TEM images of graphene. [Photoresist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photoresist "Photoresist") residue, which must be removed to obtain atomic-resolution images, may be the "[adsorbates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adsorbate "Adsorbate")" observed in TEM images, and may explain the observed rippling.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-69)

The hexagonal structure is also seen in [scanning tunneling microscope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscope "Scanning tunneling microscope") (STM) images of graphene supported on silicon dioxide substrates[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ishi2007-70) The rippling seen in these images is caused by the conformation of graphene to the substrates' lattice and is not intrinsic.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ishi2007-70)

[Ab initio calculations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ab_initio_quantum_chemistry_methods "Ab initio quantum chemistry methods") show that a graphene sheet is thermodynamically unstable if its size is less than about 20 nm and becomes the most stable [fullerene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fullerene "Fullerene") (as within graphite) only for molecules larger than 24,000 atoms.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-shen2006-71)

Electronic properties
---------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Electronic properties")]

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Electronic_band_structure_of_graphene.svg/220px-Electronic_band_structure_of_graphene.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Electronic_band_structure_of_graphene.svg)

Electronic band structure of graphene. Valence and conduction bands meet at the six vertices of the hexagonal Brillouin zone and form linearly dispersing Dirac cones.

Graphene is a zero-gap [semiconductor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiconductor "Semiconductor") because its conduction and [valence bands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valence_bands "Valence bands") meet at the [Dirac points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_cone "Dirac cone"). The Dirac points are six locations in [momentum space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Momentum_space "Momentum space") on the edge of the [Brillouin zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brillouin_zone "Brillouin zone"), divided into two non-equivalent sets of three points. These sets are labeled K and K'. These sets give graphene a [valley degeneracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valleytronics "Valleytronics") of ![Image 16: {\displaystyle g_{v}=2}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43688890a046ebb289733f87bff56e908bd17186). In contrast, for traditional semiconductors, the primary point of interest is generally Γ, where momentum is zero.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-coop2012-60)

If the in-plane direction is confined rather than infinite, its electronic structure changes. These confined structures are referred to as [graphene nanoribbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbon "Graphene nanoribbon"). If the nanoribbon has a "zig-zag" edge, the bandgap remains zero. If it has an "armchair" edge, the bandgap is non-zero.

Graphene's honeycomb structure can be viewed as two interleaving triangular lattices. This perspective has been used to calculate the band structure for a single graphite layer using a tight-binding approximation.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-coop2012-60)

### Electronic spectrum

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Electronic spectrum")]

Electrons propagating through the graphene honeycomb lattice effectively lose their mass, producing [quasi-particles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quasi-particle "Quasi-particle") described by a 2D analogue of the [Dirac equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_equation "Dirac equation") rather than the [Schrödinger equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation "Schrödinger equation") for spin-⁠1/2⁠ particles.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Castro-72)[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-E-Phonon-73)

### Dispersion relation

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Dispersion relation")]

Electronic band structure and Dirac cones, with effect of [doping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor) "Doping (semiconductor)")[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

The cleavage technique led directly to the first observation of the anomalous quantum Hall effect in graphene in 2005 by Geim's group and by [Philip Kim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philip_Kim_(physicist) "Philip Kim (physicist)") and [Yuanbo Zhang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuanbo_Zhang "Yuanbo Zhang"). This effect provided direct evidence of graphene's theoretically predicted [Berry's phase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berry%27s_phase "Berry's phase") of massless [Dirac fermions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_fermion "Dirac fermion") and proof of the Dirac fermion nature of electrons.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2005-32)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhang2005-34) These effects were previously observed in bulk graphite by Yakov Kopelevich, Igor A. Luk'yanchuk, and others, in 2003–2004.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kope2003-74)[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-luky2004-75)

When atoms are placed onto the graphene hexagonal lattice, the overlap between the _p_ z(π) orbitals and the _s_ or the _p_ x and _p_ y orbitals is zero by symmetry. Therefore, _p_ z electrons forming the π bands in graphene can be treated independently. Within this π-band approximation, using a conventional [tight-binding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tight-binding "Tight-binding") model, the [dispersion relation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dispersion_relation "Dispersion relation") (restricted to first-nearest-neighbor interactions only) that produces the energy of the electrons with wave vector _k_ is:[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Semenoff-30)[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Wallace-76)

![Image 17: {\displaystyle E(k_{x},k_{y})=\pm \,\gamma _{0}{\sqrt {1+4\cos ^{2}{{\tfrac {1}{2}}ak_{x}}+4\cos {{\tfrac {1}{2}}ak_{x}}\cdot \cos {{\tfrac {\sqrt {3}}{2}}ak_{y}}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b645b1f886d6174807ef41a2bc8782006d86ee05)
with the nearest-neighbor (π orbitals) hopping energy _γ_ 0 ≈ 2.8 eV and the [lattice constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_constant "Lattice constant")_a_ ≈ 2.46 Å. The conduction and valence bands correspond to the different signs. With one _p_ z electron per atom in this model, the valence band is fully occupied, while the conduction band is vacant. The two bands touch at the zone corners (the _K_ point in the Brillouin zone), where there is a zero density of states but no band gap. Thus, graphene exhibits a semi-metallic (or zero-gap semiconductor) character, although this is not true for a graphene sheet rolled into a [carbon nanotube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_nanotube "Carbon nanotube") due to its curvature. Two of the six Dirac points are independent, while the rest are equivalent by symmetry. Near the _K_-points, the energy depends _linearly_ on the wave vector, similar to a relativistic particle.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Semenoff-30)[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-CBE-77) Since an elementary cell of the lattice has a basis of two atoms, the [wave function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wave_function "Wave function") has an effective [2-spinor structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spinor "Spinor").

Consequently, at low energies even neglecting the true spin, electrons can be described by an equation formally equivalent to the massless [Dirac equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_equation "Dirac equation"). Hence, the electrons and holes are called Dirac [fermions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermions "Fermions").[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Semenoff-30) This pseudo-relativistic description is restricted to the [chiral limit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chirality_(physics) "Chirality (physics)"), i.e., to vanishing rest mass _M_ 0, leading to interesting additional features:[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Semenoff-30)[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-cabra2-78)

![Image 18: {\displaystyle v_{F}\,{\vec {\sigma }}\cdot \nabla \psi (\mathbf {r} )\,=\,E\psi (\mathbf {r} ).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e0a23b5dd2bb320309ff36e29ac045c4973d827f)
Here _v F_ ~ 10 6 m/s (.003 c) is the [Fermi velocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi_velocity "Fermi velocity") in graphene, which replaces the velocity of light in the Dirac theory; ![Image 19: {\displaystyle {\vec {\sigma }}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e2fc4b8da9e315ad0648308ed315c4087ec679c2) is the vector of the [Pauli matrices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pauli_matrices "Pauli matrices"), ![Image 20: {\displaystyle \psi (\mathbf {r} )}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a5942742e26cc0a33549c3cb11c79e14f76fcfa6) is the two-component wave function of the electrons, and _E_ is their energy.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Castro-72)

The equation describing the electrons' linear dispersion relation is:

![Image 21: {\displaystyle E(q)=\hbar v_{F}q}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2132c16e41dabd692c8f12c00d5dc8ffeee610e8)
where the [wavevector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavevector "Wavevector")_q_ is measured from the Brillouin zone vertex K, ![Image 22: {\displaystyle q=\left|\mathbf {k} -\mathrm {K} \right|}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4c6978c8fe843e6c5d7f7f9a5270ab440cca6774), and the zero of energy is set to coincide with the Dirac point. The equation uses a pseudospin matrix formula that describes two sublattices of the honeycomb lattice.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-CBE-77)

### Single-atom wave propagation

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Single-atom wave propagation")]

Electron waves in graphene propagate within a single-atom layer, making them sensitive to the proximity of other materials such as [high-κ dielectrics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-%CE%BA_dielectric "High-κ dielectric"), [superconductors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superconductor "Superconductor"), and [ferromagnets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferromagnets "Ferromagnets").

### Ambipolar electron and hole transport

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Ambipolar electron and hole transport")]

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Graphene_-_Geim_-_ambipolar_FET.svg/220px-Graphene_-_Geim_-_ambipolar_FET.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_-_Geim_-_ambipolar_FET.svg)

When the gate voltage in a field effect graphene device is changed from positive to negative, conduction switches from electrons to holes. The charge carrier concentration is proportional to the applied voltage. Graphene is neutral at zero gate voltage and resistivity is at its maximum because of the dearth of charge carriers. The rapid fall of resistivity when carriers are injected shows their high mobility, here of the order of 5000 cm 2/Vs. n-Si/SiO 2 substrate, T=1K.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)

Graphene exhibits high [electron mobility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_mobility "Electron mobility") at room temperature, with values reported in excess of 15 000 cm 2⋅V−1⋅s−1.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2) Hole and electron mobilities are nearly identical.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-E-Phonon-73) The mobility is independent of temperature between 10 K and 100 K,[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2005-32)[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-GiantMobility-79)[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-E-ph-80) showing minimal change even at room temperature (300 K),[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2) suggesting that the dominant scattering mechanism is [defect scattering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Defect_scattering "Defect scattering"). Scattering by graphene's acoustic [phonons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phonon "Phonon") intrinsically limits room temperature mobility in freestanding graphene to 200 000 cm 2⋅V−1⋅s−1 at a carrier density of 10 12 cm−2.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-E-ph-80)[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-GrapheneMC-81)

The corresponding [resistivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Resistivity "Resistivity") of graphene sheets is 10−8 Ω⋅m, lower than the resistivity of [silver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silver "Silver"), which is the lowest known at room temperature.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-UMDnews-82) However, on SiO

2 substrates, electron scattering by optical phonons of the substrate has a more significant effect than scattering by graphene's phonons, limiting mobility to 40 000 cm 2⋅V−1⋅s−1.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-E-ph-80)

Charge transport can be affected by the adsorption of contaminants such as [water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water "Water") and [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") molecules, leading to non-repetitive and large hysteresis I-V characteristics. Researchers need to conduct electrical measurements in a vacuum. Coating the graphene surface with materials such as SiN, [PMMA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poly(methyl_methacrylate) "Poly(methyl methacrylate)") or h-BN has been proposed for protection. In January 2015, the first stable graphene device operation in the air over several weeks was reported for graphene whose surface was protected by [aluminum oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aluminium_oxide "Aluminium oxide").[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-83)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-84) In 2015, [lithium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithium "Lithium")-coated graphene exhibited [superconductivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superconductivity "Superconductivity"), a first for graphene.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-85)

Electrical resistance in 40-nanometer-wide [nanoribbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoribbon "Nanoribbon") of epitaxial graphene changes in discrete steps. The ribbons' conductance exceeds predictions by a factor of 10. The ribbons can function more like [optical waveguides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_waveguide "Optical waveguide") or [quantum dots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_dot "Quantum dot"), allowing electrons to flow smoothly along the ribbon edges. In copper, resistance increases proportionally with length as electrons encounter impurities.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-k1402-86)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-doi_12952-87)

Transport is dominated by two modes: one ballistic and temperature-independent, and the other thermally activated. Ballistic electrons resemble those in cylindrical carbon nanotubes. At room temperature, resistance increases abruptly at a specific length—the ballistic mode at 16 micrometers and the thermally activated mode at 160 nanometers (1% of the former length).[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-k1402-86)

Graphene electrons can traverse micrometer distances without scattering, even at room temperature.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Castro-72)

#### Electrical conductivity and charge transport

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Electrical conductivity and charge transport")]

Despite zero carrier density near the Dirac points, graphene exhibits a minimum conductivity on the order of ![Image 24: {\displaystyle 4e^{2}/h}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/415a87049948e893336c8e064298fcd6ade13d78). The origin of this minimum conductivity is still unclear. However, rippling of the graphene sheet or ionized impurities in the SiO

2 substrate may lead to local puddles of carriers that allow conduction.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-E-Phonon-73) Several theories suggest that the minimum conductivity should be ![Image 25: {\displaystyle 4e^{2}/{(\pi }h)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b9f82b5b3f048886efb7c0e658f260e8be11cb37); however, most measurements are of the order of ![Image 26: {\displaystyle 4e^{2}/h}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/415a87049948e893336c8e064298fcd6ade13d78) or greater[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2) and depend on impurity concentration.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-K-88)

Near zero carrier density, graphene exhibits positive photoconductivity and negative photoconductivity at high carrier density, governed by the interplay between photoinduced changes of both the [Drude weight](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Drude_weight&action=edit&redlink=1 "Drude weight (page does not exist)") and the carrier scattering rate.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-89)

Graphene doped with various gaseous species (both acceptors and donors) can be returned to an undoped state by gentle heating in a vacuum.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-K-88)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ChemDoping-90) Even for [dopant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dopant "Dopant") concentrations in excess of 10 12 cm−2, carrier mobility exhibits no observable change.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ChemDoping-90) Graphene doped with [potassium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium "Potassium") in [ultra-high vacuum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultra-high_vacuum "Ultra-high vacuum") at low temperature can reduce mobility 20-fold.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-K-88)[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-GrapheneCharge-91) The mobility reduction is reversible on heating the graphene to remove the potassium.

Due to graphene's two dimensions, charge fractionalization (where the apparent charge of individual pseudoparticles in low-dimensional systems is less than a single quantum)[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-92) is thought to occur. It may therefore be a suitable material for constructing [quantum computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_computer "Quantum computer")[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-93) using [anyonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anyon "Anyon") circuits.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-94)

### Chiral half-integer quantum Hall effect

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Chiral half-integer quantum Hall effect")]

[![Image 27](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Graphene_-_Geim_-_Landau_levels.svg/150px-Graphene_-_Geim_-_Landau_levels.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_-_Geim_-_Landau_levels.svg)

Landau levels in graphene appear at energies proportional to √_N_, in contrast to the standard sequence that goes as _N_ + ⁠1/2⁠.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)

#### Quantum hall effect in graphene

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Quantum hall effect in graphene")]

The [quantum Hall effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_Hall_effect "Quantum Hall effect") is a quantum mechanical version of the [Hall effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hall_effect "Hall effect"), which is the production of transverse (perpendicular to the main current) conductivity in the presence of a [magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field "Magnetic field"). The quantization of the [Hall effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hall_effect "Hall effect")![Image 28: {\displaystyle \sigma _{xy}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ecff8b738ef9b15f1bdbe014e4d19e8d2414d0b) at integer multiples (the "[Landau level](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landau_level "Landau level")") of the basic quantity _e_ 2/_h_ (where _e_ is the elementary electric charge and _h_ is the [Planck constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planck_constant "Planck constant")). It can usually be observed only in very clean [silicon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon "Silicon") or [gallium arsenide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallium_arsenide "Gallium arsenide") solids at temperatures around 3[K](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kelvin "Kelvin") and very high magnetic fields.

Graphene shows the quantum Hall effect: the conductivity quantization is unusual in that the sequence of steps is shifted by 1/2 with respect to the standard sequence and with an additional factor of 4. Graphene's Hall conductivity is ![Image 29: {\displaystyle \sigma _{xy}=\pm {4\cdot \left(N+1/2\right)e^{2}}/h}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fe534d928156654281476587b11adc43812d413), where _N_ is the Landau level and the double valley and double spin degeneracies give the factor of 4.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2) These anomalies are present not only at extremely low temperatures but also at room temperature, i.e. at roughly 20°C (293 K).[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2005-32)

#### Chiral electrons and anomalies

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Chiral electrons and anomalies")]

This behavior is a direct result of graphene's chiral, massless Dirac electrons.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-95) In a magnetic field, their spectrum has a Landau level with energy precisely at the Dirac point. This level is a consequence of the [Atiyah–Singer index theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atiyah%E2%80%93Singer_index_theorem "Atiyah–Singer index theorem") and is half-filled in neutral graphene,[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Semenoff-30) leading to the "+1/2" in the Hall conductivity.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-gusy2005-33)[Bilayer graphene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bilayer_graphene "Bilayer graphene") also shows the quantum Hall effect, but with only one of the two anomalies (i.e. ![Image 30: {\displaystyle \sigma _{xy}=\pm {4\cdot N\cdot e^{2}}/h}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5c5d583e0dc9f40597250c321ea6beb4e2c267ab)). In the second anomaly, the first plateau at _N_ = 0 is absent, indicating that bilayer graphene stays metallic at the neutrality point.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)

[![Image 31](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Graphene_-_Geim_-_Chiral_half-integer_quantum_Hall_effect.svg/220px-Graphene_-_Geim_-_Chiral_half-integer_quantum_Hall_effect.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_-_Geim_-_Chiral_half-integer_quantum_Hall_effect.svg)

[Chiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry) "Chirality (chemistry)") half-integer quantum Hall effect in graphene. Plateaux in transverse conductivity appear at half-integer multiples of 4 _e_ 2/_h_.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)

Unlike normal metals, graphene's longitudinal resistance shows maxima rather than minima for integral values of the Landau filling factor in measurements of the [Shubnikov–de Haas oscillations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shubnikov%E2%80%93de_Haas_effect "Shubnikov–de Haas effect"), thus the term "_integral_ quantum Hall effect". These oscillations show a phase shift of π, known as [Berry's phase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometric_phase "Geometric phase").[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2005-32)[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-E-Phonon-73) Berry's phase arises due to chirality or dependence (locking) of the pseudospin quantum number on the momentum of low-energy electrons near the Dirac points.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhang2005-34) The temperature dependence of the oscillations reveals that the carriers have a non-zero cyclotron mass, despite their zero effective mass in the Dirac-fermion formalism.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-novo2005-32)

#### Experimental observations

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Experimental observations")]

Graphene samples prepared on nickel films, and on both the silicon face and carbon face of [silicon carbide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon_carbide#Structure_and_properties "Silicon carbide"), show the anomalous effect directly in electrical measurements.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ByungHeeHong-96)[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-0908.1900-97)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ShenAPL-98)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-0909.2903-99)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-0909.1193-100)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-phase1-101) Graphitic layers on the carbon face of silicon carbide show a clear [Dirac spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_spectrum "Dirac spectrum") in [angle-resolved photoemission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPES "ARPES") experiments, and the effect is observed in cyclotron resonance and tunneling experiments.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Fuhrer09-102)

### "Massive" electrons

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: \"Massive\" electrons")]

Graphene's unit cell has two identical carbon atoms and two zero-energy states: one where the electron resides on atom A, and the other on atom B. However, if the unit cell's two atoms are not identical, the situation changes. Research shows that placing [hexagonal boron nitride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boron_nitride "Boron nitride") (h-BN) in contact with graphene can alter the potential felt at atoms A and B sufficiently for the electrons to develop a mass and an accompanying band gap of about 30 meV.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-sci1306-103)

The mass can be positive or negative. An arrangement that slightly raises the energy of an electron on atom A relative to atom B gives it a positive mass, while an arrangement that raises the energy of atom B produces a negative electron mass. The two versions behave alike and are indistinguishable via [optical spectroscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_spectroscopy "Optical spectroscopy"). An electron traveling from a positive-mass region to a negative-mass region must cross an intermediate region where its mass once again becomes zero. This region is gapless and therefore metallic. Metallic modes bounding semiconducting regions of opposite-sign mass is a hallmark of a [topological phase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topological_phase "Topological phase") and displays much the same physics as topological insulators.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-sci1306-103)

If the mass in graphene can be controlled, electrons can be confined to massless regions by surrounding them with massive regions, allowing the patterning of [quantum dots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_dot "Quantum dot"), wires, and other mesoscopic structures. It also produces one-dimensional conductors along the boundary. These wires would be protected against [backscattering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Backscatter "Backscatter") and could carry currents without dissipation.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-sci1306-103)

### Inertial effect and kinetic inductance

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Inertial effect and kinetic inductance")]

While a single electron exhibits zero effective mass in graphene, as the entire set of electrons is moved with an electric field, the Fermi disk shifts, with both the total kinetic energy ![Image 32: {\displaystyle E}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b) and the magnitude of the total momentum ![Image 33: {\displaystyle P}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a) being increased. Since ![Image 34: {\displaystyle E}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b) is minimum when ![Image 35: {\displaystyle P=0}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f6f743f37b37ce0c2ddc1db0fdca0e577c19f51d), to the first order, ![Image 36: {\displaystyle E\sim P^{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5a7c854ab3a0cd54eb44823633f362729ff4c705) must hold.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kinetic-104) The proportional constant must be given by ![Image 37: {\displaystyle 1/(2M)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e9e7737427e5d2b3f31d888077c3a28b20ba2a4d) where ![Image 38: {\displaystyle M}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f82cade9898ced02fdd08712e5f0c0151758a0dd) is the collective mass (inertia) of graphene electrons. Another view of this is to associate the total kinetic energy with the current. Because the total momentum is proportional to the current ![Image 39: {\displaystyle I}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/535ea7fc4134a31cbe2251d9d3511374bc41be9f), ![Image 40: {\displaystyle E\sim I^{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/612c8da79b7e3d9324c7ba8e520f3980b20cee4a) must hold. The proportional constant must be given by ![Image 41: {\displaystyle (1/2)L_{k}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e6623e4880e0e86e4378c0cb7789e6656d5d6110), where ![Image 42: {\displaystyle L_{k}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/939365b07ac6bf3558837d0c457ca855dcb5e7d4) is a quantity of inductance unit, but its origin is not the Faraday induction but the inertial effect. ![Image 43: {\displaystyle L_{k}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/939365b07ac6bf3558837d0c457ca855dcb5e7d4) is the graphene kinetic inductance[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kinetic-104), and is given by ![Image 44: {\displaystyle L_{K}=(\pi \hbar ^{2})/(e^{2}\epsilon _{F}W)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/88abb79b115ca98f491ea9eda1f839673e840ac7) per unit length for a graphene strip with a width ![Image 45: {\displaystyle W}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54a9c4c547f4d6111f81946cad242b18298d70b7) (![Image 46: {\displaystyle \epsilon _{F}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9577888ef778a0bf205a7df5647d6953873a0561) is the Fermi energy). The kinetic inductance, representing the collective inertial effect, is intimately linked to graphene plasmonics, and was directly measured via microwave network analysis in 2014.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kinetic-104)

Interactions and phenomena
--------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Interactions and phenomena")]

### Strong magnetic fields

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Strong magnetic fields")]

In magnetic fields above 10 [tesla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla_(unit) "Tesla (unit)"), additional plateaus of the Hall conductivity at _σ_ _xy_ = _νe_ 2/_h_ with _ν_ = 0, ±1, ±4 are observed.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-0-1-4-105) A plateau at _ν_ = 3[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-3-106) and the [fractional quantum Hall effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fractional_quantum_Hall_effect "Fractional quantum Hall effect") at _ν_ = ⁠1/3⁠ were also reported.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-3-106)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-one3rd-107)

These observations with _ν_ = 0, ±1, ±3, ±4 indicate that the four-fold degeneracy (two valley and two spin degrees of freedom) of the Landau energy levels is partially or completely lifted.

The [Casimir effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Casimir_effect "Casimir effect") is an interaction between disjoint neutral bodies provoked by the fluctuations of the electromagnetic vacuum. Mathematically, it can be explained by considering the normal modes of electromagnetic fields, which explicitly depend on the boundary conditions on the interacting bodies' surfaces. Due to graphene's strong interaction with the electromagnetic field as a one-atom-thick material, the Casimir effect has garnered significant interest.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-BFGV-108)[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-FMD-109)

### Van der Waals force

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Van der Waals force")]

The [Van der Waals force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force "Van der Waals force") (or dispersion force) is also unusual, obeying an inverse cubic asymptotic [power law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_law "Power law") in contrast to the usual inverse quartic law.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-DWR-110)

Graphene's [permittivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permittivity "Permittivity") varies with frequency. Over a range from microwave to millimeter wave frequencies, it is approximately 3.3.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-cismaru-111) This permittivity, combined with its ability to function as both a conductor and as an insulator, theoretically allows compact [capacitors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitor "Capacitor") made of graphene to store large amounts of electrical energy.

Graphene exhibits unique optical properties, showing unexpectedly high [opacity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opacity_(optics) "Opacity (optics)") for an atomic monolayer in vacuum, absorbing approximately _πα_ ≈ 2.3% of [light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light "Light") from visible to infrared wavelengths,[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nair2008-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhu2014-6)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-112) where _α_ is the [fine-structure constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fine-structure_constant "Fine-structure constant"). This is due to the unusual low-energy electronic structure of monolayer graphene, characterized by electron and hole [conical bands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conical_intersection "Conical intersection") meeting at the [Dirac point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#Electronic_properties), which is qualitatively different from more common quadratic massive bands.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nair2008-5) Based on the Slonczewski–Weiss–McClure (SWMcC) band model of graphite, calculations using [Fresnel equations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fresnel_equations "Fresnel equations") in the thin-film limit account for interatomic distance, hopping values, and frequency, thus assessing optical conductance.

Experimental verification, though confirmed, lacks the precision required to improve upon existing techniques for determining the [fine-structure constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fine-structure_constant "Fine-structure constant").[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-113)

### Multi-parametric surface plasmon resonance

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Multi-parametric surface plasmon resonance")]

[Multi-parametric surface plasmon resonance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multi-parametric_surface_plasmon_resonance "Multi-parametric surface plasmon resonance") has been utilized to characterize both the thickness and refractive index of chemical-vapor-deposition (CVD)-grown graphene films. At a wavelength of 670[nm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanometre "Nanometre") (6.7×10−7[m](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre "Metre")), measured refractive index and extinction coefficient values are 3.135 and 0.897, respectively. Thickness determination yielded 3.7 Å across a 0.5mm area, consistent with the 3.35 Å reported for layer-to-layer carbon atom distance of graphite crystals.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-114) This method is applicable for real-time label-free interactions of graphene with organic and inorganic substances. The existence of unidirectional surface plasmons in nonreciprocal graphene-based gyrotropic interfaces has been theoretically demonstrated, offering tunability from THz to near-infrared and visible frequencies by controlling graphene's chemical potential.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-115) Particularly, the unidirectional frequency bandwidth can be 1– 2 orders of magnitude larger than that achievable with metal under similar magnetic field conditions, stemming from graphene's extremely small effective electron mass.

### Tunable band gap and optical response

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Tunable band gap and optical response")]

Graphene's [band gap](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Band_gap "Band gap") can be tuned from 0 to 0.25 eV (about 5-micrometer wavelength) by applying a voltage to a dual-gate [bilayer graphene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bilayer_graphene "Bilayer graphene")[field-effect transistor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Field-effect_transistor "Field-effect transistor") (FET) at room temperature.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-116) The optical response of [graphene nanoribbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbons "Graphene nanoribbons") is tunable into the [terahertz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terahertz_radiation "Terahertz radiation") regime by an applied magnetic fields.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-117) Graphene/graphene oxide systems exhibit [electrochromic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrochromic "Electrochromic") behavior, enabling tuning of both linear and ultrafast optical properties.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kurum2011-118)

### Graphene-based Bragg grating

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Graphene-based Bragg grating")]

A graphene-based [Bragg grating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bragg_grating "Bragg grating") (one-dimensional [photonic crystal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photonic_crystal "Photonic crystal")) has been fabricated, demonstrating its capability to excite surface electromagnetic waves in periodic structure using a 633[nm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanometre "Nanometre") (6.33×10−7[m](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre "Metre")) He–Ne laser as the light source.[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-119)

### Saturable absorption

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Saturable absorption")]

Graphene exhibits unique saturable absorption, which saturates when the input optical intensity exceeds a threshold value. This nonlinear optical behavior, termed [saturable absorption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saturable_absorption "Saturable absorption"), occurs across the visible to [near-infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-infrared "Near-infrared") spectrum, due to graphene's universal optical absorption and zero band gap. This property has enabled full-band mode-locking in [fiber lasers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiber_laser "Fiber laser") using graphene-based saturable absorbers, contributing significantly to ultrafast [photonics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photonics "Photonics"). Additionally, the optical response of graphene/graphene oxide layers can be electrically tuned.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kurum2011-118)[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-bao2009-120)[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhang2009a-121)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhang2009b-122)[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhang2010a-123)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zhang2009c-124)

Saturable absorption in graphene could occur at the Microwave and Terahertz band, owing to its wideband optical absorption property. The microwave-saturable absorption in graphene demonstrates the possibility of graphene microwaves and terahertz photonics devices, such as a microwave-saturable absorber, modulator, polarizer, microwave signal processing, and broadband wireless access networks.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zheng2012-125)

### Nonlinear Kerr effect

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Nonlinear Kerr effect")]

Under intense laser illumination, graphene exhibits a nonlinear phase shift due to the optical nonlinear [Kerr effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kerr_effect "Kerr effect"). Graphene demonstrates a large nonlinear Kerr coefficient of 10−7 cm 2⋅W−1, nearly nine orders of magnitude larger than that of bulk dielectrics,[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ZHANGHAN-126) suggesting its potential as a powerful nonlinear Kerr medium capable of supporting various nonlinear effects, including [solitons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soliton "Soliton").[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-127)

Excitonic properties
--------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Excitonic properties")]

First-principle calculations incorporating quasiparticle corrections and many-body effects have been employed to study the electronic and optical properties of graphene-based materials. The approach was described as three stages.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-128) With GW calculation, the properties of graphene-based materials were accurately investigated, including bulk graphene,[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-129)[nanoribbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbons "Graphene nanoribbons"),[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-130) edge and surface functionalized armchair ribbons,[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-131) hydrogen saturated armchair ribbons,[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-132)[Josephson effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Josephson_effect "Josephson effect") in graphene SNS junctions with single localized defect[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-133) and armchair ribbon scaling properties.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-134)

Graphene is considered an ideal material for [spintronics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spintronics "Spintronics") due to its minimal [spin–orbit interaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin%E2%80%93orbit_interaction "Spin–orbit interaction"), the near absence of [nuclear magnetic moments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_moment "Nuclear magnetic moment") in carbon, and weak [hyperfine interaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperfine_interaction "Hyperfine interaction"). Electrical injection and detection of [spin current](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_current "Spin current") have been demonstrated up to room temperature,[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Tombros-135)[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ChoSpin-136)[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Ohishi-137) with spin coherence length exceeding 1 micrometer observed at this temperature.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Tombros-135) Control of spin current polarity via electrical gating has been achieved at low temperatures.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ChoSpin-136)

Magnetic properties
-------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Magnetic properties")]

### Strong magnetic fields

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Strong magnetic fields")]

Graphene's quantum Hall effect in magnetic fields above approximately 10 [tesla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla_(unit) "Tesla (unit)") reveals additional interesting features. Additional plateaus in Hall conductivity at ![Image 47: {\displaystyle \sigma _{xy}=\nu e^{2}/h}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5b8a85afc35897e198803b5a2892e3f5fe4f3906) with ![Image 48: {\displaystyle \nu =0,\pm {1},\pm {4}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e9e9532133e4bb5d7a3c63fd3047b566a5b32154) have been observed,[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-0-1-4-105) along with plateau at ![Image 49: {\displaystyle \nu =3}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ff23f7aea3cc2a731be7831d061dea5df292e8b)[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-3-106) and a fractional quantum Hall effect at ![Image 50: {\displaystyle \nu =1/3}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/101bc977fd38e9b455e8a1998bf5ccb9c2370199).[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-3-106)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nu-one3rd-107)

These observations with ![Image 51: {\displaystyle \nu =0,\pm 1,\pm 3,\pm 4}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ca372041704064c85569c632bfd7ae44a83b30e0) indicate that the four-fold degeneracy (two valley and two spin degrees of freedom) of the Landau energy levels is partially or completely lifted. One hypothesis proposes that [magnetic catalysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_catalysis "Magnetic catalysis") of [symmetry breaking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry_breaking "Symmetry breaking") is responsible for this degeneracy lift.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

### Spintronic properties

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Spintronic properties")]

Graphene exhibits [spintronic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spintronics "Spintronics") and magnetic properties concurrently.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-138) Low-defect graphene Nano-meshes, fabricated using a non-lithographic approach, exhibit significant ferromagnetism even at room temperature. Additionally, a spin pumping effect has been observed with fields applied in parallel to the planes of few-layer ferromagnetic nano-meshes, while a [magnetoresistance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetoresistance "Magnetoresistance") hysteresis loop is evident under perpendicular fields. Charge-neutral graphene has demonstrated magnetoresistance exceeding 100% in magnetic fields generated by standard permanent magnets (approximately 0.1 tesla), marking a record magneto resistivity _at room temperature_ among known materials.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-139)

### Magnetic substrates

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Magnetic substrates")]

In 2010, researchers magnetized graphene by producing it via CVD on the Ni(111) substrate[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-140) and then in 2014 by placing it on an atomically smooth layer of magnetic [yttrium iron garnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yttrium_iron_garnet "Yttrium iron garnet"), maintaining graphene's electronic properties unaffected. Previous methods involved doping graphene with other substances.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-141) The dopant's presence negatively affected its electronic properties.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-142)

Mechanical properties
---------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Mechanical properties")]

As noted [above](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#Structure), the (two-dimensional) density of graphene is 0.762 mg per square meter.

Graphene is the strongest material ever tested,[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lee2008-7)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-cao2020-8) with an intrinsic [tensile strength](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tensile_strength "Tensile strength") of 130[GPa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (19,000,000[psi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch "Pound per square inch")) (with representative engineering tensile strength ~50-60 GPa for stretching large-area freestanding graphene) and a [Young's modulus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young%27s_modulus "Young's modulus") (stiffness) close to 1[TPa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (150,000,000[psi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch "Pound per square inch")). The Nobel announcement illustrated this by saying that a 1 square meter graphene hammock would support a 4 kg cat but would weigh only as much as one of the cat's whiskers, at 0.77 mg (about 0.001% of the weight of 1 m 2 of paper).[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nobelprize.org-143)

Large-angle bending of graphene monolayers with minimal strain demonstrates its mechanical robustness. Even under extreme deformation, monolayer graphene maintains excellent carrier mobility.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-144)

The [spring constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spring_constant "Spring constant") of suspended graphene sheets has been measured using an [atomic force microscope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_force_microscope "Atomic force microscope") (AFM). Graphene sheets were suspended over SiO
