2 cavities where an AFM tip was used to apply stress to the sheet to test its mechanical properties. Its spring constant was in the range 1–5 N/m and the stiffness was 0.5 TPa, which differs from that of bulk graphite. These intrinsic properties could lead to applications such as [NEMS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoelectromechanical_systems "Nanoelectromechanical systems") as pressure sensors and resonators.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-145) Due to its large surface energy and out of plane ductility, flat graphene sheets are unstable with respect to scrolling, i.e. bending into a cylindrical shape, which is its lower-energy state.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nmscrolling-146)

In two-dimensional structures like graphene, thermal and quantum fluctuations cause relative displacement, with fluctuations growing logarithmically with structure size as per the [Mermin–Wagner theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mermin%E2%80%93Wagner_theorem "Mermin–Wagner theorem"). This shows that the amplitude of long-wavelength fluctuations grows logarithmically with the scale of a 2D structure, and would therefore be unbounded in structures of infinite size. Local deformation and elastic strain are negligibly affected by this long-range divergence in relative displacement. It is believed that a sufficiently large 2D structure, in the absence of applied lateral tension, will bend and crumple to form a fluctuating 3D structure. Researchers have observed ripples in suspended layers of graphene,[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-meyer2007-36) and it has been proposed that the ripples are caused by thermal fluctuations in the material. As a consequence of these dynamical deformations, it is debatable whether graphene is truly a 2D structure.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2007-2)[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-carl2007-67)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-faso2007-68)[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-bolm2011a-147)[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-bolm2011b-148) These ripples, when amplified by vacancy defects, induce a negative [Poisson's ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisson%27s_ratio "Poisson's ratio") into graphene, resulting in the thinnest [auxetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auxetics "Auxetics") material known so far.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-grima2014-149)

Graphene-nickel (Ni) composites, created through plating processes, exhibit enhanced mechanical properties due to strong Ni-graphene interactions inhibiting dislocation sliding in the Ni matrix.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-150)

In 2014, researchers from [Rice University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice_University "Rice University") and the [Georgia Institute of Technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_Institute_of_Technology "Georgia Institute of Technology") have indicated that despite its strength, graphene is also relatively [brittle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brittle "Brittle"), with a [fracture toughness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fracture_toughness "Fracture toughness") of about 4 MPa√m.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ZhangMa2014-151) This indicates that imperfect graphene is likely to crack in a brittle manner like [ceramic materials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceramic#Mechanical_properties "Ceramic"), as opposed to many [metallic materials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metal#Form_and_structure "Metal") which tend to have fracture toughness in the range of 15–50 MPa√m. Later in 2014, the Rice team announced that graphene showed a greater ability to distribute force from an impact than any known material, ten times that of steel per unit weight.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-152) The force was transmitted at 22.2 kilometres per second (13.8 mi/s).[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-153)

### Polycrystalline graphene

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Polycrystalline graphene")]

Various methods – most notably, [chemical vapor deposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition "Chemical vapor deposition") (CVD), as discussed in the section below – have been developed to produce large-scale graphene needed for device applications. Such methods often synthesize polycrystalline graphene.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:5-154) The mechanical properties of polycrystalline graphene are affected by the nature of the defects, such as [grain-boundaries (GB)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grain_boundary "Grain boundary") and [vacancies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vacancy_defect "Vacancy defect"), present in the system and the average grain-size.

Graphene grain boundaries typically contain heptagon-pentagon pairs. The arrangement of such defects depends on whether the GB is in a zig-zag or armchair direction. It further depends on the tilt-angle of the GB.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-155) In 2010, researchers from [Brown University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_University "Brown University") computationally predicted that as the tilt-angle increases, the grain boundary strength also increases. They showed that the weakest link in the grain boundary is at the critical bonds of the heptagon rings. As the grain boundary angle increases, the strain in these heptagon rings decreases, causing the grain boundary to be stronger than lower-angle GBs. They proposed that, in fact, for sufficiently large angle GB, the strength of the GB is similar to pristine graphene.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-156) In 2012, it was further shown that the strength can increase or decrease, depending on the detailed arrangements of the defects.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-157) These predictions have since been supported by experimental evidence. In a 2013 study led by James Hone's group, researchers probed the elastic [stiffness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stiffness "Stiffness") and [strength](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strength_of_materials "Strength of materials") of CVD-grown graphene by combining nano-indentation and high-resolution [TEM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy "Transmission electron microscopy"). They found that the elastic stiffness is identical and strength is only slightly lower than those in pristine graphene.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-158) In the same year, researchers from [University of California, Berkeley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley "University of California, Berkeley") and [University of California, Los Angeles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles "University of California, Los Angeles") probed bi-crystalline graphene with [TEM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy "Transmission electron microscopy") and [AFM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_force_microscopy "Atomic force microscopy"). They found that the strength of grain boundaries indeed tends to increase with the tilt angle.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-159)

While the presence of vacancies is not only prevalent in polycrystalline graphene, vacancies can have significant effects on the strength of graphene. The consensus is that the strength decreases along with increasing densities of vacancies. Various studies have shown that for graphene with a sufficiently low density of vacancies, the strength does not vary significantly from that of pristine graphene. On the other hand, a high density of vacancies can severely reduce the strength of graphene.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:7-160)

Compared to the fairly well-understood nature of the effect that grain boundary and vacancies have on the mechanical properties of graphene, there is no clear consensus on the general effect that the average grain size has on the strength of polycrystalline graphene.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:6-161)[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:7-160)[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:8-162) In fact, three notable theoretical or computational studies on this topic have led to three different conclusions.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:9-163)[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:10-164)[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:11-165) First, in 2012, Kolakowski and Myer studied the mechanical properties of polycrystalline graphene with "realistic atomistic model", using [molecular-dynamics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_dynamics "Molecular dynamics") (MD) simulation. To emulate the growth mechanism of CVD, they first randomly selected [nucleation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleation "Nucleation") sites that are at least 5A (arbitrarily chosen) apart from other sites. Polycrystalline graphene was generated from these nucleation sites and was subsequently annealed at 3000K, and then quenched. Based on this model, they found that cracks are initiated at grain-boundary junctions, but the grain size does not significantly affect the strength.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:9-163) Second, in 2013, Z. Song et al. used MD simulations to study the mechanical properties of polycrystalline graphene with uniform-sized hexagon-shaped grains. The hexagon grains were oriented in various lattice directions and the GBs consisted of only heptagon, pentagon, and hexagonal carbon rings. The motivation behind such a model was that similar systems had been experimentally observed in graphene flakes grown on the surface of liquid copper. While they also noted that crack is typically initiated at the triple junctions, they found that as the grain size decreases, the yield strength of graphene increases. Based on this finding, they proposed that polycrystalline follows pseudo [Hall-Petch relationship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hall-Petch_relationship "Hall-Petch relationship").[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:10-164) Third, in 2013, Z. D. Sha et al. studied the effect of grain size on the properties of polycrystalline graphene, by modeling the grain patches using [Voronoi construction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voronoi_diagram "Voronoi diagram"). The GBs in this model consisted of heptagons, pentagons, and hexagons, as well as squares, octagons, and vacancies. Through MD simulation, contrary to the aforementioned study, they found an inverse Hall-Petch relationship, where the strength of graphene increases as the grain size increases.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:11-165) Experimental observations and other theoretical predictions also gave differing conclusions, similar to the three given above.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:8-162) Such discrepancies show the complexity of the effects that grain size, arrangements of defects, and the nature of defects have on the mechanical properties of polycrystalline graphene.

### Thermal conductivity

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Thermal conductivity")]

Thermal transport in graphene is a burgeoning area of research, particularly for its potential applications in thermal management. Most experimental measurements have posted large uncertainties in the results of thermal conductivity due to the limitations of the instruments used. Following predictions for graphene and related [carbon nanotubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_nanotubes "Carbon nanotubes"),[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-DT130-166) early measurements of the [thermal conductivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_conductivity "Thermal conductivity") of suspended graphene reported an exceptionally large thermal conductivity up to 5300 W⋅m−1⋅K−1,[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Balandin-167) compared with the thermal conductivity of pyrolytic [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite") of approximately 2000 W⋅m−1⋅K−1 at room temperature.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Touloukian1970-168) However, later studies primarily on more scalable but more defected graphene derived by Chemical Vapor Deposition have been unable to reproduce such high thermal conductivity measurements, producing a wide range of thermal conductivities between 1500 – 2500 W⋅m−1⋅K−1 for suspended single-layer graphene.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-CaiMoore2010-169)[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-FaugerasFaugeras2010-170)[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-XuPereira2014-171)[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-LeeYoon2011-172) The large range in the reported thermal conductivity can be caused by large measurement uncertainties as well as variations in the graphene quality and processing conditions. In addition, it is known that when single-layer graphene is supported on an amorphous material, the thermal conductivity is reduced to about 500 – 600 W⋅m−1⋅K−1 at room temperature as a result of scattering of graphene lattice waves by the substrate,[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-SeolJo2010-173)[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Klemens2001-174) and can be even lower for few-layer graphene encased in amorphous oxide.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-JangChen2010-175) Likewise, polymeric residue can contribute to a similar decrease in the thermal conductivity of suspended graphene to approximately 500 – 600 W⋅m−1⋅K−1 for bilayer graphene.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-PettesJo2011-176)

Isotopic composition, specifically the ratio of [12 C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon-12 "Carbon-12") to [13 C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon-13 "Carbon-13"), significantly affects graphene's thermal conductivity. Isotopically pure 12 C graphene exhibits higher thermal conductivity than either a 50:50 isotope ratio or the naturally occurring 99:1 ratio.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-chen2012natmat-177) It can be shown by using the [Wiedemann–Franz law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wiedemann%E2%80%93Franz_law "Wiedemann–Franz law"), that the thermal conduction is [phonon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phonon "Phonon")-dominated.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Balandin-167) However, for a gated graphene strip, an applied gate bias causing a [Fermi energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi_energy "Fermi energy") shift much larger than _k_ B _T_ can cause the electronic contribution to increase and dominate over the [phonon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phonon "Phonon") contribution at low temperatures. The ballistic thermal conductance of graphene is isotropic.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Saito2-178)[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Qizhen_Liang,_Xuxia_Yao,_Wei_Wang,_Yan_Liu,_Ching_Ping_Wong._2011_2392%E2%80%932401-179)

Graphite, a 3D counterpart to graphene, exhibits a [basal plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basal_plane "Basal plane")[thermal conductivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_conductivity "Thermal conductivity") exceeding 1000 W⋅m−1⋅K−1 (similar to [diamond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamond "Diamond")), In graphite, the c-axis (out of plane) thermal conductivity is over a factor of ~100 smaller due to the weak binding forces between basal planes as well as the larger [lattice spacing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_spacing "Lattice spacing").[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-180) In addition, the ballistic thermal conductance of graphene is shown to give the lower limit of the ballistic thermal conductance, per unit circumference, length of carbon nanotubes.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-mingo-181)

Graphene's thermal conductivity is influenced by its three [acoustic phonon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acoustic_phonon "Acoustic phonon") modes: two linear [dispersion relation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dispersion_relation "Dispersion relation") dispersion relation in-plane modes (LA, TA) and one quadratic dispersion relation out-of-plane mode (ZA). At low temperatures, the dominance of the T 1.5 thermal conductivity contribution of the out-of-plane mode supersedes the _T_ 2 dependence of the linear modes.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-mingo-181) Some graphene phonon bands exhibit negative [Grüneisen parameters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gr%C3%BCneisen_parameter "Grüneisen parameter"),[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-mounet-182) resulting in negative [thermal expansion coefficient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_expansion_coefficient "Thermal expansion coefficient") at low temperatures. The lowest negative Grüneisen parameters correspond to the lowest transverse acoustic ZA modes, whose frequencies increase with in-plane [lattice parameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_parameter "Lattice parameter"), akin to a stretched string with higher frequency vibrations.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lifshitz-183)

### Chemical properties

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Chemical properties")]

Graphene has a theoretical [specific surface area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Specific_surface_area "Specific surface area") (SSA) of 2630[m 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_metre "Square metre")/g. This is much larger than that reported to date for carbon black (typically smaller than 900[m 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_metre "Square metre")/g) or for carbon nanotubes (CNTs), from ≈100 to 1000[m 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_metre "Square metre")/g and is similar to [activated carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Activated_carbon "Activated carbon").[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-bonaccorso2015-184) Graphene is the only form of carbon (or solid material) in which every atom is available for chemical reaction from two sides (due to the 2D structure). Atoms at the edges of a graphene sheet have special chemical reactivity. Graphene has the highest ratio of edge atoms of any [allotrope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allotrope "Allotrope"). Defects within a sheet increase its chemical reactivity.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-denis2013-185) The onset temperature of reaction between the basal plane of single-layer graphene and oxygen gas is below 260°C (530 K).[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Yamada3-186) Graphene burns at very low temperatures (e.g., 350°C (620 K)).[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Eftekhari-187) Graphene is commonly modified with oxygen- and nitrogen-containing [functional groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Functional_group "Functional group") and analyzed by [infrared spectroscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy "Infrared spectroscopy") and [X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray_photoelectron_spectroscopy "X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy"). However, the determination of structures of graphene with oxygen-[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Yamada1-188) and nitrogen-[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Yamada2-189) functional groups require the structures to be well controlled.

In 2013, [Stanford University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University") physicists reported that single-layer graphene is a hundred times more chemically reactive than thicker multilayer sheets.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-190)

Graphene can self-repair holes in its sheets, when exposed to molecules containing carbon, such as [hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrocarbon "Hydrocarbon"). Bombarded with pure carbon atoms, the atoms perfectly align into [hexagons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon"), filling the holes.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-zan2012-191)[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-puiu2012-192)

### Biological properties

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Biological properties")]

Despite the promising results in different cell studies and proof of concept studies, there is still incomplete understanding of the full biocompatibility of graphene-based materials.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-193) Different cell lines react differently when exposed to graphene, and it has been shown that the lateral size of the graphene flakes, the form and surface chemistry can elicit different biological responses on the same cell line.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-194)

There are indications that graphene has promise as a useful material for interacting with neural cells; studies on cultured neural cells show limited success.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-195)[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-196)

Graphene also has some utility in [osteogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osteogenic "Osteogenic"). Researchers at the Graphene Research Centre at the [National University of Singapore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_University_of_Singapore "National University of Singapore") (NUS) discovered in 2011 the ability of graphene to accelerate the osteogenic differentiation of human [mesenchymal stem cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesenchymal_stem_cell "Mesenchymal stem cell") without the use of biochemical inducers.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-197)

Graphene can be used in biosensors; in 2015, researchers demonstrated that a graphene-based sensor can be used to detect a cancer risk biomarker. In particular, by using epitaxial graphene on silicon carbide, they were repeatedly able to detect 8-hydroxydeoxyguanosine (8-OHdG), a DNA damage biomarker.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-198)

The electronic property of graphene can be significantly influenced by the supporting substrate. Studies of graphene monolayers on clean and hydrogen(H)-passivated silicon (100) (Si(100)/H) surfaces have been performed.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-199) The Si(100)/H surface does not perturb the electronic properties of graphene, whereas the interaction between the clean Si(100) surface and graphene changes the electronic states of graphene significantly. This effect results from the covalent bonding between C and surface Si atoms, modifying the π-orbital network of the graphene layer. The local density of states shows that the bonded C and Si surface states are highly disturbed near the Fermi energy.

Graphene layers and structural variants
---------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=47 "Edit section: Graphene layers and structural variants")]

In 2013 a group of Polish scientists presented a production unit that allows the manufacture of continuous monolayer sheets.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-200) The process is based on graphene growth on a liquid metal matrix.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-201) The product of this process was called [High Strength Metallurgical Graphene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HSMG "HSMG"). In a new study published in Nature, the researchers have used a single-layer graphene electrode and a novel surface-sensitive non-linear spectroscopy technique to investigate the top-most water layer at the electrochemically charged surface. They found that the interfacial water response to the applied electric field is asymmetric concerning the nature of the applied field.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-202)

Bilayer graphene displays the [anomalous quantum Hall effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anomalous_quantum_Hall_effect "Anomalous quantum Hall effect"), a tunable [band gap](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Band_gap "Band gap")[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-PRB75.155115-203) and potential for [excitonic condensation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exciton#Interaction "Exciton")[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-PRL104.096802-204)–making it a promising candidate for [optoelectronic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optoelectronic "Optoelectronic") and [nanoelectronic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoelectronic "Nanoelectronic") applications. Bilayer graphene typically can be found either in [twisted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twistronics "Twistronics") configurations where the two layers are rotated relative to each other or graphitic Bernal stacked configurations where half the atoms in one layer lie atop half the atoms in the other.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nl204547v-205) Stacking order and orientation govern the optical and electronic properties of bilayer graphene.

One way to synthesize bilayer graphene is via [chemical vapor deposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition "Chemical vapor deposition"), which can produce large bilayer regions that almost exclusively conform to a Bernal stack geometry.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-nl204547v-205)

It has been shown that the two graphene layers can withstand important strain or doping mismatch[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-206) which ultimately should lead to their exfoliation.

Turbostratic graphene exhibits weak interlayer coupling, and the spacing is increased with respect to Bernal-stacked multilayer graphene. Rotational misalignment preserves the 2D electronic structure, as confirmed by [Raman spectroscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy "Raman spectroscopy").[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-turbostratic-raman-207) The D peak is very weak, whereas the 2D and G peaks remain prominent.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-turbostratic-raman-207)

A rather peculiar feature is that the I 2D/I G ratio can exceed 10. However, most importantly, the M peak, which originates from AB stacking, is absent, whereas the TS 1 and TS 2 modes are visible in the [Raman spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raman_spectrum "Raman spectrum").[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Luong_647%E2%80%93651-208)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-209) The material is formed through conversion of non-graphenic carbon into graphenic carbon without providing sufficient energy to allow for the reorganization through annealing of adjacent graphene layers into crystalline graphitic structures.

### Graphene superlattices

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=51 "Edit section: Graphene superlattices")]

Periodically stacked graphene and its insulating isomorph provide a fascinating structural element in implementing highly functional superlattices at the atomic scale, which offers possibilities for designing nanoelectronic and photonic devices. Various types of superlattices can be obtained by stacking graphene and its related forms.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-210) The energy band in layer-stacked superlattices is found to be more sensitive to the barrier width than that in conventional III–V semiconductor superlattices. When adding more than one atomic layer to the barrier in each period, the coupling of electronic wavefunctions in neighboring potential wells can be significantly reduced, which leads to the degeneration of continuous subbands into quantized energy levels. When varying the well width, the energy levels in the potential wells along the L-M direction behave distinctly from those along the K-H direction.

A superlattice corresponds to a periodic or quasi-periodic arrangement of different materials and can be described by a superlattice period which confers a new translational symmetry to the system, impacting their phonon dispersions and subsequently their thermal transport properties. Recently, uniform monolayer graphene-hBN structures have been successfully synthesized via lithography patterning coupled with chemical vapor deposition (CVD).[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-211) Furthermore, superlattices of graphene-HBN are ideal model systems for the realization and understanding of coherent (wave-like) and incoherent (particle-like) phonon thermal transport.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-212)[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-213)[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-214)[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-215)[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-216)

Nanostructured graphene forms
-----------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=52 "Edit section: Nanostructured graphene forms")]

### Graphene nanoribbons

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=53 "Edit section: Graphene nanoribbons")]

[![Image 52](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Graphene_edge_names.svg/330px-Graphene_edge_names.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_edge_names.svg)

Names for graphene edge topologies

[![Image 53](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Cnt_zz_v3.gif/500px-Cnt_zz_v3.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Cnt_zz_v3.gif)

[GNR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbon "Graphene nanoribbon") Electronic band structure of graphene strips of varying widths in zig-zag orientation. Tight-binding calculations show that they are all metallic.

[![Image 54](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif/500px-Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif)

[GNR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbon "Graphene nanoribbon") Electronic band structure of graphene strips of various widths in the armchair orientation. Tight-binding calculations show that they are semiconducting or metallic depending on width (chirality).

[Graphene nanoribbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbons "Graphene nanoribbons") ("nanostripes" in the "zig-zag"/"zigzag" orientation), at low temperatures, show spin-polarized metallic edge currents, which also suggests applications in the new field of [spintronics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spintronics "Spintronics"). (In the "armchair" orientation, the edges behave like semiconductors.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Castro-72))

### Graphene quantum dots

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=54 "Edit section: Graphene quantum dots")]

A [graphene quantum dot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_quantum_dot "Graphene quantum dot") (GQD) is a graphene fragment with a size lesser than 100 nm. The properties of GQDs are different from bulk graphene due to the quantum confinement effects which only become apparent when the size is smaller than 100 nm.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-tang2014-217)[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-tang2012-218)[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-tang2013-219)

Modified and functionalized graphene
------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=55 "Edit section: Modified and functionalized graphene")]

Graphene oxide is usually produced through chemical exfoliation of graphite. A particularly popular technique is the improved [Hummers' method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hummers%27_method "Hummers' method").[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-220) Using paper-making techniques on dispersed, oxidized and chemically processed graphite in water, the monolayer flakes form a single sheet and create strong bonds. These sheets, called [graphene oxide paper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_oxide_paper "Graphene oxide paper"), have a measured [tensile modulus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tensile_modulus "Tensile modulus") of 32 [GPa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPa "GPa").[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-221) The chemical property of graphite oxide is related to the functional groups attached to graphene sheets. These can change the [polymerization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymerization "Polymerization") pathway and similar chemical processes.[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-222) Graphene oxide flakes in [polymerss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymers "Polymers") display enhanced photo-conducting properties.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-223) Graphene is normally [hydrophobic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrophobe "Hydrophobe") and impermeable to all gases and liquids (vacuum-tight). However, when formed into a graphene oxide-based capillary membrane, both liquid water and water vapor flow through as quickly as if the membrane were not present.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-pmid22282806-224)

In 2022, researchers evaluated the biological effects of low doses on graphene oxide on larvae and imago of _[Drosophila melanogaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drosophila\_melanogaster "Drosophila melanogaster")_. Results show that oral administration of graphene oxide at concentrations of 0.02-1% has a beneficial effect on the developmental rate and hatching ability of larvae. Long-term administration of a low dose of graphene oxide extends the lifespan of Drosophila and significantly enhances resistance to environmental stresses. These suggest that graphene oxide affects carbohydrate and lipid metabolism in adult Drosophila. These findings might provide a useful reference to assess the biological effects of graphene oxide, which could play an important role in a variety of graphene-based biomedical applications.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Evaluation_of_biological_effects_of_graphene_oxide_using_Drosophila-225)

### Chemical modification

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=57 "Edit section: Chemical modification")]

[![Image 55](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/70/Slgo.jpg/330px-Slgo.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Slgo.jpg)

Photograph of single-layer graphene oxide undergoing high temperature chemical treatment, resulting in sheet folding and loss of carboxylic functionality, or through room temperature carbodiimide treatment, collapsing into star-like clusters.

Soluble fragments of graphene can be prepared in the laboratory through chemical modification of graphite.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-226) First, microcrystalline graphite is treated with an acidic mixture of sulfuric acid and [nitric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_acid "Nitric acid"). A series of oxidation and exfoliation steps produce small graphene plates with [carboxyl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carboxyl "Carboxyl") groups at their edges. These are converted to [acid chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_chloride "Acid chloride") groups by treatment with [thionyl chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thionyl_chloride "Thionyl chloride"); next, they are converted to the corresponding graphene [amide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amide "Amide") via treatment with octadecyl amine. The resulting material (circular graphene layers of 5.3[Å](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m "Ångström") or 5.3×10−10[m](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre "Metre") thickness) is soluble in [tetrahydrofuran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran "Tetrahydrofuran"), [tetrachloromethane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrachloromethane "Tetrachloromethane") and [dichloroethane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1,2-Dichloroethane "1,2-Dichloroethane").

Refluxing single-layer graphene oxide (SLGO) in [solvents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solvent "Solvent") leads to size reduction and folding of individual sheets as well as loss of carboxylic group functionality, by up to 20%, indicating thermal instabilities of SLGO sheets dependent on their preparation methodology. When using thionyl chloride, [acyl chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acyl_chloride "Acyl chloride") groups result, which can then form aliphatic and aromatic amides with a reactivity conversion of around 70–80%.

[![Image 56](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5f/Graphene_chemistry.jpg/500px-Graphene_chemistry.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Graphene_chemistry.jpg)

Boehm titration results for various chemical reactions of single-layer graphene oxide, which reveal reactivity of the carboxylic groups and the resultant stability of the SLGO sheets after treatment.

[Hydrazine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrazine "Hydrazine") reflux is commonly used for reducing SLGO to SLG(R), but [titrations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titration "Titration") show that only around 20–30% of the carboxylic groups are lost, leaving a significant number available for chemical attachment. Analysis of SLG(R) generated by this route reveals that the system is unstable and using a room temperature stirring with [hydrochloric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid "Hydrochloric acid") (< 1.0 M) leads to around 60% loss of COOH functionality. Room temperature treatment of SLGO with [carbodiimides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbodiimide "Carbodiimide") leads to the collapse of the individual sheets into star-like clusters that exhibited poor subsequent reactivity with amines (c. 3–5% conversion of the intermediate to the final amide).[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-227) It is apparent that conventional chemical treatment of carboxylic groups on SLGO generates morphological changes of individual sheets that leads to a reduction in chemical reactivity, which may potentially limit their use in composite synthesis. Therefore, chemical reaction types have been explored. SLGO has also been grafted with [polyallylamine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyallylamine_hydrochloride "Polyallylamine hydrochloride"), cross-linked through [epoxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epoxy "Epoxy") groups. When filtered into graphene oxide paper, these composites exhibit increased stiffness and strength relative to unmodified graphene oxide paper.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-228)

Full [hydrogenation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogenation "Hydrogenation") from both sides of the graphene sheet results in [Graphane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphane "Graphane"), but partial hydrogenation leads to hydrogenated graphene.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-229) Similarly, both-side fluorination of graphene (or chemical and mechanical exfoliation of graphite fluoride) leads to [fluorographene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluorographene "Fluorographene") (graphene fluoride),[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-230) while partial fluorination (generally [halogenation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halogenation "Halogenation")) provides fluorinated (halogenated) graphene.

### Graphene ligand/complex

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=58 "Edit section: Graphene ligand/complex")]

Graphene can be a [ligand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ligand "Ligand") to [coordinate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coordinate_covalent_bond "Coordinate covalent bond") metals and metal ions by introducing functional groups. Structures of graphene ligands are similar to e.g. metal-[porphyrin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Porphyrin "Porphyrin") complex, metal-[phthalocyanine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phthalocyanine "Phthalocyanine") complex, and metal-[phenanthroline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenanthroline "Phenanthroline") complex. Copper and nickel ions can be coordinated with graphene ligands.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-231)[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-232)

Advanced graphene structures
----------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=59 "Edit section: Advanced graphene structures")]

In 2011, researchers reported a novel yet simple approach to fabricating graphene fibers from chemical vapor deposition-grown graphene films.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-233) The method was scalable and controllable, delivering tunable morphology and pore structure by controlling the evaporation of solvents with suitable [surface tension](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_tension "Surface tension"). Flexible all-solid-state supercapacitors based on these graphene fibers were demonstrated in 2013.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-234)

In 2015, intercalating small graphene fragments into the gaps formed by larger, coiled graphene sheets, after annealing provided pathways for conduction, while the fragments helped reinforce the fibers.[_[sentence fragment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sentence\_fragment "Sentence fragment")_] The resulting fibers offered better thermal and electrical conductivity and mechanical strength. Thermal conductivity reached 1,290[W](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Watt "Watt")/[m](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre "Metre")/[K](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kelvin "Kelvin") (1,290 watts per metre per kelvin), while tensile strength reached 1,080[MPa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (157,000[psi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch "Pound per square inch")).[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-235)

In 2016, kilometer-scale continuous graphene fibers with outstanding mechanical properties and excellent electrical conductivity were produced by high-throughput wet-spinning of graphene oxide liquid crystals followed by [graphitization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphitization "Graphitization") through a full-scale synergetic defect-engineering strategy.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-236) The graphene fibers with superior performances promise wide applications in functional textiles, lightweight motors, microelectronic devices, etc.

[Tsinghua University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsinghua_University "Tsinghua University") in Beijing, led by Wei Fei of the Department of Chemical Engineering, claims to be able to create a carbon nanotube fiber that has a tensile strength of 80[GPa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal_(unit) "Pascal (unit)") (12,000,000[psi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch "Pound per square inch")).[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-237)

In 2013, a three-dimensional [honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honeycomb "Honeycomb") of hexagonally arranged carbon was termed 3D graphene, and self-supporting 3D graphene was also produced.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-238) 3D structures of graphene can be fabricated by using either CVD or solution-based methods. A 2016 review by Khurram and Xu et al. provided a summary of then-state-of-the-art techniques for fabrication of the 3D structure of graphene and other related two-dimensional materials.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-auto-239) In 2013, researchers at [Stony Brook University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stony_Brook_University "Stony Brook University") reported a novel radical-initiated crosslinking method to fabricate porous 3D free-standing architectures of graphene and carbon nanotubes using nanomaterials as building blocks without any polymer matrix as support.[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-PMID_23436939-240) These 3D graphenes (all-carbon) scaffolds/foams have applications in several fields such as energy storage, filtration, thermal management, and biomedical devices and implants.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-auto-239)[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-PMID_25788440-241)

Box-shaped graphene (BSG) [nanostructure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanostructure "Nanostructure") appearing after mechanical cleavage of [pyrolytic graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrolytic_graphite "Pyrolytic graphite") was reported in 2016.[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-stm2016-242) The discovered nanostructure is a multilayer system of parallel hollow nanochannels located along the surface and having quadrangular cross-section. The thickness of the channel walls is approximately equal to 1 nm. Potential fields of BSG application include ultra-sensitive [detectors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Detector "Detector"), high-performance catalytic cells, nanochannels for [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA")[sequencing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequencing "Sequencing") and manipulation, high-performance heat sinking surfaces, [rechargeable batteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rechargeable_battery "Rechargeable battery") of enhanced performance, [nanomechanical resonators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanomechanical_resonator "Nanomechanical resonator"), electron multiplication channels in emission [Nano-electronic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoelectronics "Nanoelectronics") devices, high-capacity [sorbents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sorbent "Sorbent") for safe [hydrogen storage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_storage "Hydrogen storage").

Three dimensional bilayer graphene has also been reported.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-243)[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-244)

Pillared graphene is a hybrid carbon structure, consisting of an oriented array of carbon nanotubes connected at each end to a sheet of graphene. It was first described theoretically by George Froudakis and colleagues at the [University of Crete](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Crete "University of Crete") in Greece in 2008. Pillared graphene has not yet been synthesized in the laboratory, but it has been suggested that it may have useful electronic properties, or as a hydrogen storage material.

### Reinforced graphene

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=63 "Edit section: Reinforced graphene")]

Graphene reinforced with embedded [carbon nanotube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_nanotube "Carbon nanotube") reinforcing bars ("rebar") is easier to manipulate, while improving the electrical and mechanical qualities of both materials.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kurz5011-245)[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-246)

Functionalized single- or multi-walled carbon nanotubes are spin-coated on copper foils and then heated and cooled, using the nanotubes themselves as the carbon source. Under heating, the functional [carbon groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_group "Carbon group") decompose into graphene, while the nanotubes partially split and form in-plane [covalent bonds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Covalent_bond "Covalent bond") with the graphene, adding strength. [π–π stacking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi_stacking "Pi stacking") domains add more strength. The nanotubes can overlap, making the material a better conductor than standard CVD-grown graphene. The nanotubes effectively bridge the [grain boundaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grain_boundary "Grain boundary") found in conventional graphene. The technique eliminates the traces of substrate on which later-separated sheets were deposited using epitaxy.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kurz5011-245)

Stacks of a few layers have been proposed as a cost-effective and physically flexible replacement for [indium tin oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide "Indium tin oxide") (ITO) used in displays and [photovoltaic cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photovoltaic_cell "Photovoltaic cell").[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kurz5011-245)

In 2015, researchers from the [University of Illinois at Urbana–Champaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana%E2%80%93Champaign "University of Illinois at Urbana–Champaign") (UIUC) developed a new approach for forming 3D shapes from flat, 2D sheets of graphene.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-247) A film of graphene that had been soaked in solvent to make it swell and become malleable was overlaid on an underlying substrate "former". The solvent evaporated over time, leaving behind a layer of graphene that had taken on the shape of the underlying structure. In this way, they were able to produce a range of relatively intricate micro-structured shapes.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-248) Features vary from 3.5 to 50 μm. Pure graphene and gold-decorated graphene were each successfully integrated with the substrate.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-249)

Specialized graphene configurations
-----------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=65 "Edit section: Specialized graphene configurations")]

An [aerogel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerogel "Aerogel") made of graphene layers separated by carbon nanotubes was measured at 0.16 milligrams per cubic centimeter. A solution of graphene and carbon nanotubes in a mold is freeze-dried to dehydrate the solution, leaving the aerogel. The material has superior elasticity and absorption. It can recover completely after more than 90% compression, and absorb up to 900 times its weight in oil, at a rate of 68.8 grams per second.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-250)

In 2015, a coiled form of graphene was discovered in graphitic carbon (coal). The spiraling effect is produced by defects in the material's hexagonal grid that causes it to spiral along its edge, mimicking a [Riemann surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_surface "Riemann surface"), with the graphene surface approximately perpendicular to the axis. When voltage is applied to such a coil, current flows around the spiral, producing a magnetic field. The phenomenon applies to spirals with either zigzag or armchair patterns, although with different current distributions. Computer simulations indicated that a conventional spiral inductor of 205 microns in diameter could be matched by a nanocoil just 70 nanometers wide, with a field strength reaching as much as 1 [tesla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla_(unit) "Tesla (unit)").[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:0-251)

The nano-solenoids analyzed through computer models at [Rice University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rice_University "Rice University") should be capable of producing powerful magnetic fields of about 1 tesla, about the same as the coils found in typical loudspeakers, according to Yakobson and his team – and about the same field strength as some MRI machines. They found the magnetic field would be strongest in the hollow, nanometer-wide cavity at the spiral's center.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:0-251)

A [solenoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solenoid "Solenoid") made with such a coil behaves as a quantum conductor whose current distribution between the core and exterior varies with applied voltage, resulting in nonlinear [inductance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inductance "Inductance").[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-252)

In 2016, [Brown University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_University "Brown University") introduced a method for "crumpling" graphene, adding wrinkles to the material on a nanoscale. This was achieved by depositing layers of graphene oxide onto a shrink film, then shrunken, with the film dissolved before being shrunken again on another sheet of film. The crumpled graphene became [superhydrophobic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultrahydrophobicity "Ultrahydrophobicity"), and when used as a battery electrode, the material was shown to have as much as a 400% increase in [electrochemical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrochemical "Electrochemical")[current density](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Current_density "Current density").[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-253)[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-254)

Mechanical synthesis
--------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=69 "Edit section: Mechanical synthesis")]

A rapidly increasing list of production techniques have been developed to enable graphene's use in commercial applications.[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-255)

Isolated 2D crystals cannot be grown via chemical synthesis beyond small sizes even in principle, because the rapid growth of [phonon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phonon "Phonon") density with increasing lateral size forces 2D crystallites to bend into the third dimension. In all cases, graphene must bond to a substrate to retain its two-dimensional shape.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-geim2009a-19)

### Bottom-up and top-down methods

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=70 "Edit section: Bottom-up and top-down methods")]

Small graphene structures, such as graphene quantum dots and nanoribbons, can be produced by "bottom-up" methods that assemble the lattice from organic molecule monomers (e. g. citric acid, glucose). "Top-down" methods, on the other hand, cut bulk graphite and graphene materials with strong chemicals (e. g. mixed acids).[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:1-256)

### Micro-mechanical cleavage

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=71 "Edit section: Micro-mechanical cleavage")]

The most famous, clean and rather straightforward method of isolating graphene sheets, called micro-mechanical cleavage or more colloquially called the scotch tape method, was introduced by Novoselov et al. in 2004, which uses [adhesive tape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adhesive_tape "Adhesive tape") to mechanically cleave high-quality [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite")[crystals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crystal "Crystal") into successively thinner platelets. Other methods do exist like exfoliation.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:1-256)

### Exfoliation techniques

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=72 "Edit section: Exfoliation techniques")]

#### Mechanical exfoliation

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=73 "Edit section: Mechanical exfoliation")]

Geim and Novoselov initially used [adhesive tape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adhesive_tape "Adhesive tape") to pull graphene sheets away from graphite. Achieving single layers typically requires multiple exfoliation steps. After exfoliation, the flakes are deposited on a silicon wafer. Crystallites larger than 1 mm and visible to the naked eye can be obtained.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-257)

As of 2014, exfoliation produced graphene with the lowest number of defects and highest electron mobility. One specific exfoliation technique involved using a [sharp single-crystal diamond wedge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wedge_based_mechanical_exfoliation "Wedge based mechanical exfoliation") to penetrate the graphite source and precisely cleave individual layers. That same year, researchers also developed liquid-phase methods, creating defect-free, unoxidized graphene-containing liquids from graphite using mixers that generate extremely high local shear rates greater than 10×10 4.[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-258)[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-259)

A 2014 study published in _Nature Materials_ demonstrated that scalable production of defect-free graphene is possible through shear exfoliation using a [high-shear mixer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-shear_mixer "High-shear mixer"). This technique can produce large quantities of few-layer graphene in solution while preserving structural integrity.[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-260) As turbulence is not necessary for mechanical exfoliation, resonant acoustic mixing or low-speed [ball milling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ball_milling "Ball milling") can also be effective in the production of high-yield and water-soluble graphene.[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-261)[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-262)

#### Liquid phase exfoliation

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=74 "Edit section: Liquid phase exfoliation")]

[Liquid phase exfoliation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liquid_phase_exfoliation "Liquid phase exfoliation") (LPE) is a relatively simple method that involves dispersing graphite in a liquid medium to produce graphene by [sonication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sonication "Sonication") or high shear mixing, followed by [centrifugation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrifugation "Centrifugation"). Restacking is an issue with this technique unless solvents with appropriate surface energy are used (e.g. NMP). Adding a [surfactant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surfactant "Surfactant") to a solvent prior to sonication prevents restacking by adsorbing to the graphene's surface. This produces a higher graphene concentration, but removing the surfactant requires chemical treatments.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-PhysTod-263)[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-264)

LPE results in nanosheets with a broad size distribution and thicknesses roughly in the range of 1-10 monolayers. However, liquid cascade centrifugation can be used to size-select the suspensions and achieve monolayer enrichment.[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-265)

Sonicating graphite at the interface of two [immiscible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miscibility "Miscibility") liquids, most notably [heptane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heptane "Heptane") and water, produced macro-scale graphene films. The graphene sheets are adsorbed to the high-energy interface between the materials and are kept from restacking. The sheets are up to about 95% transparent and conductive.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-266)

With definite cleavage parameters, the box-shaped graphene (BSG) [nanostructure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanostructure "Nanostructure") can be prepared on [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite")[crystal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crystal "Crystal").[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-stm2016-242) A major advantage of LPE is that it can be used to exfoliate many inorganic 2D materials beyond graphene, e.g. BN, MoS2, WS2.[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-267)

#### Exfoliation with supercritical carbon dioxide

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=75 "Edit section: Exfoliation with supercritical carbon dioxide")]

Liquid-phase exfoliation can also be done by a less-known process of intercalating [supercritical carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide "Supercritical carbon dioxide") (scCO2) into the interstitial spaces in the graphite lattice, followed by rapid depressurization. The scCO2 intercalates easily inside the graphite lattice at a pressure of roughly 100 [atm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atm_(unit) "Atm (unit)"). Carbon dioxide turns gaseous as soon as the vessel is depressurized and makes the graphite explode into few-layered graphene.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:1-256)

This method may have multiple advantages: being non-toxic, the graphite does not have to be chemically treated in any way before the process, and the whole process can be completed in a single step as opposed to other exfoliation methods.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:1-256)

### Splitting monolayer carbon allotropes

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=76 "Edit section: Splitting monolayer carbon allotropes")]

Graphene can be created by opening [carbon nanotubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_nanotube "Carbon nanotube") by cutting or etching.[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-268) In one such method, [multi-walled carbon nanotubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MWNT "MWNT") were cut open in solution by action of [potassium permanganate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium_permanganate "Potassium permanganate") and [sulfuric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfuric_acid "Sulfuric acid").[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-269)[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-270) In 2014, carbon nanotube-reinforced graphene was made via spin coating and annealing functionalized carbon nanotubes.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-kurz5011-245)

Another approach sprays [buckyballs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buckminsterfullerene "Buckminsterfullerene") at supersonic speeds onto a substrate. The balls cracked open upon impact, and the resulting unzipped cages then bond together to form a graphene film.[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-271)

### Graphite oxide reduction

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=78 "Edit section: Graphite oxide reduction")]

P. Boehm reported producing monolayer flakes of reduced graphene oxide in 1962.[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-grtim2009-272)[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-273) Rapid heating of graphite oxide and exfoliation yields highly dispersed carbon powder with a few percent of graphene flakes.

Another method is the reduction of graphite oxide monolayer films, e.g. by [hydrazine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrazine "Hydrazine") with [annealing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy) "Annealing (metallurgy)") in [argon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argon "Argon")/[hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen") with an almost intact carbon framework that allows efficient removal of functional groups. Measured [charge carrier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charge_carrier "Charge carrier") mobility exceeded 1,000 cm/Vs (10 m/Vs).[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Eigler2013-274)

Burning a graphite oxide coated [DVD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DVD "DVD") produced a conductive graphene film (1,738 siemens per meter) and specific surface area (1,520 square meters per gram) that was highly resistant and malleable.[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-275)

A dispersed reduced graphene oxide suspension was synthesized in water by a hydrothermal dehydration method without using any surfactant. The approach is facile, industrially applicable, environmentally friendly, and cost-effective. Viscosity measurements confirmed that the graphene colloidal suspension (graphene nanofluid) exhibits Newtonian behavior, with the viscosity showing a close resemblance to that of water.[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-276)

Graphite particles can be corroded in molten salts to form a variety of carbon nanostructures including graphene.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-277) Hydrogen cations, dissolved in molten lithium chloride, can be discharged on cathodically polarized graphite rods, which then intercalate, peeling graphene sheets. The graphene nanosheets produced displayed a single-crystalline structure with a lateral size of several hundred nanometers and a high degree of crystallinity and thermal stability.[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-278)

### Electrochemical synthesis

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=80 "Edit section: Electrochemical synthesis")]

Electrochemical synthesis can exfoliate graphene. Varying a pulsed voltage controls thickness, flake area, and number of defects and affects its properties. The process begins by bathing the graphite in a solvent for intercalation. The process can be tracked by monitoring the solution's transparency with an LED and photodiode.[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-279)[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-280)

### Hydrothermal self-assembly

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=81 "Edit section: Hydrothermal self-assembly")]

Graphene has been prepared by using a sugar like [glucose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glucose "Glucose"), [fructose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fructose "Fructose"), etc. This substrate-free "bottom-up" synthesis is safer, simpler and more environmentally friendly than exfoliation. The method can control the thickness, ranging from monolayer to multilayer, which is known as the "Tang-Lau Method".[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-r1-281)[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lixu2013-282)[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lili2013b-283)[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lixu2014-284)

### Sodium ethoxide pyrolysis

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=82 "Edit section: Sodium ethoxide pyrolysis")]

Gram-quantities were produced by the reaction of [ethanol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethanol "Ethanol") with [sodium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium "Sodium") metal, followed by [pyrolysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrolysis "Pyrolysis") and washing with water.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-285)

### Microwave-assisted oxidation

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=83 "Edit section: Microwave-assisted oxidation")]

In 2012, microwave energy was reported to directly synthesize graphene in one step.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-286) This approach avoids use of potassium permanganate in the reaction mixture. It was also reported that by microwave radiation assistance, graphene oxide with or without holes can be synthesized by controlling microwave time.[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-287) Microwave heating can dramatically shorten the reaction time from days to seconds.

Graphene can also be made by [microwave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microwave_radiation "Microwave radiation") assisted hydrothermal pyrolysis.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-tang2014-217)[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-tang2012-218)

### Thermal decomposition of silicon carbide

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=84 "Edit section: Thermal decomposition of silicon carbide")]

Heating [silicon carbide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon_carbide "Silicon carbide") (SiC) to high temperatures (1100°C) under low pressures (c. 10−6 torr, or 10−4 Pa) reduces it to graphene.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-0908.1900-97)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ShenAPL-98)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-0909.2903-99)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-0909.1193-100)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-phase1-101)[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-288)

Vapor deposition and growth techniques
--------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=85 "Edit section: Vapor deposition and growth techniques")]

### Chemical vapor deposition

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=86 "Edit section: Chemical vapor deposition")]

[Epitaxial graphene growth on silicon carbide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epitaxial_graphene_growth_on_silicon_carbide "Epitaxial graphene growth on silicon carbide") is a wafer-scale technique to produce graphene. [Epitaxial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epitaxy "Epitaxy") graphene may be coupled to surfaces weakly enough (by the active valence electrons that create [Van der Waals forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force "Van der Waals force")) to retain the two-dimensional [electronic band structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_band_structure "Electronic band structure") of isolated graphene.[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Gall1-289)

A normal [silicon wafer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon_wafer "Silicon wafer") coated with a layer of [germanium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanium "Germanium") (Ge) dipped in dilute [hydrofluoric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid "Hydrofluoric acid") strips the naturally forming [germanium oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanium_oxide "Germanium oxide") groups, creating hydrogen-terminated germanium. CVD can coat that with graphene.[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-290)[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-291)

The direct synthesis of graphene on insulator TiO 2 with high-dielectric-constant (high-κ). A two-step CVD process is shown to grow graphene directly on TiO 2 crystals or exfoliated TiO 2 nanosheets without using any metal catalyst.[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-292)

CVD graphene can be grown on metal substrates including [ruthenium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruthenium "Ruthenium"),[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-PhysOrg.com-293)[iridium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iridium "Iridium"),[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-grIr111-294)[nickel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nickel "Nickel")[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-295) and [copper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copper "Copper").[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-296)[[297]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-297)

In 2014, a two-step roll-to-roll manufacturing process was announced. The first roll-to-roll step produces the graphene via chemical vapor deposition. The second step binds the graphene to a substrate.[[298]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-298)[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-299)

[![Image 57](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Wafer_Scale_CVD_Graphene_Raman_Mapping.gif/330px-Wafer_Scale_CVD_Graphene_Raman_Mapping.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Wafer_Scale_CVD_Graphene_Raman_Mapping.gif)

Large-area Raman mapping of CVD graphene on deposited Cu thin film on 150 mm SiO 2/Si wafers reveals >95% monolayer continuity and an average value of ~2.62 for _I_ 2D/_I_ G. The scale bar is 200 μm.

Growing graphene in an industrial resistive-heating cold wall CVD system was claimed to produce graphene 100 times faster than conventional CVD systems, cut costs by 99%, and produce material with enhanced electronic qualities.[[300]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-300)[[301]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-301)

#### Wafer scale CVD graphene

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=91 "Edit section: Wafer scale CVD graphene")]

CVD graphene is scalable and has been grown on deposited Cu thin film catalyst on 100 to 300 mm standard Si/SiO 2 wafers[[302]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-302)[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:3-303)[[304]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:4-304) on an Axitron Black Magic system. Monolayer graphene coverage of >95% is achieved on 100 to 300 mm wafer substrates with negligible defects, confirmed by extensive Raman mapping.[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:3-303)[[304]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-:4-304)

### Solvent interface trapping method (SITM)

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=92 "Edit section: Solvent interface trapping method (SITM)")]

As reported by a group led by D. H. Adamson, graphene can be produced from natural graphite while preserving the integrity of the sheets using the solvent interface trapping method (SITM). SITM uses a high-energy interface, such as oil and water, to exfoliate graphite to graphene. Stacked graphite delaminates, or spreads, at the oil/water interface to produce few-layer graphene in a thermodynamically favorable process in much the same way as small molecule surfactants spread to minimize the interfacial energy. In this way, graphene behaves like a 2D surfactant.[[305]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-305)[[306]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-306)[[307]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-307) SITM has been reported for a variety of applications such conductive polymer-graphene foams,[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-308)[[309]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-309)[[310]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-310)[[311]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-311) conductive polymer-graphene microspheres,[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-312) conductive thin films[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-313) and conductive inks.[[314]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-314)

### Carbon dioxide reduction

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=93 "Edit section: Carbon dioxide reduction")]

A highly exothermic reaction combusts [magnesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium") in an oxidation-reduction reaction with carbon dioxide, producing carbon nanoparticles including graphene and [fullerenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fullerene "Fullerene").[[315]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-315)

Supersonic acceleration of droplets through a [Laval nozzle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laval_nozzle "Laval nozzle") was used to deposit reduced graphene oxide on a substrate. The energy of the impact rearranges those carbon atoms into flawless graphene.[[316]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-316)[[317]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-317)

In 2014, a CO

2[infrared laser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared_laser "Infrared laser") was used to produce patterned porous three-dimensional laser-induced graphene (LIG) film networks from commercial polymer films. The resulting material exhibits high electrical conductivity and surface area. The laser induction process is compatible with roll-to-roll manufacturing processes.[[318]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-318) A similar material, laser-induced graphene fibers (LIGF), was reported in 2018.[[319]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-319)

### Flash Joule heating

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=96 "Edit section: Flash Joule heating")]

In 2019, flash Joule heating (transient high-temperature electrothermal heating) was discovered to be a method to synthesize turbostratic graphene in bulk powder form. The method involves electrothermally converting various carbon sources, such as carbon black, coal, and food waste into micron-scale flakes of graphene.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-Luong_647%E2%80%93651-208)[[320]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-320) More recent works demonstrated the use of mixed [plastic waste](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plastic_waste "Plastic waste"), waste rubber tires, and pyrolysis ash as carbon feedstocks.[[321]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-321)[[322]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-322)[[323]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-323) The graphenization process is kinetically controlled, and the energy dose is chosen to preserve the carbon in its graphenic state (excessive energy input leads to subsequent graphitization through annealing).

Accelerating carbon ions inside an electrical field into a semiconductor made of thin nickel films on a substrate of SiO 2/Si, creates a wafer-scale (4 inches (100 mm)) wrinkle/tear/residue-free graphene layer at a relatively low temperature of 500°C.[[324]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-324)[[325]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-325)

### CMOS-compatible graphene

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&action=edit&section=98 "Edit section: CMOS-compatible graphene")]

Integration of graphene in the widely employed [CMOS fabrication process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiconductor_device_fabrication "Semiconductor device fabrication") demands its transfer-free direct synthesis on [dielectric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dielectric "Dielectric") substrates at temperatures below 500°C. At the [IEDM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Electron_Devices_Meeting "International Electron Devices Meeting") 2018, researchers from [University of California, Santa Barbara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Barbara "University of California, Santa Barbara"), demonstrated a novel CMOS-compatible graphene synthesis process at 300°C suitable for back-end-of-line ([BEOL](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Back_end_of_line "Back end of line")) applications.[[326]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-326)[[327]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-327)[[328]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-328) The process involves pressure-assisted solid-state [diffusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffusion "Diffusion") of [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon") through a [thin-film](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thin_film "Thin film") of metal catalyst. The synthesized large-area graphene films were shown to exhibit high quality (via [Raman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy "Raman spectroscopy") characterization) and similar [resistivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity "Electrical resistivity and conductivity") values when compared with high-temperature CVD synthesized graphene films of the same cross-section down to widths of 20 [nm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanometre "Nanometre").
