In addition to experimental investigation of graphene and graphene-based devices, numerical modeling and simulation of graphene has also been an important research topic. The [Kubo formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kubo_formula "Kubo formula") provides an analytic expression for the graphene's conductivity and shows that it is a function of several physical parameters including wavelength, temperature, and chemical potential.[[329]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-329) Moreover, a surface conductivity model, which describes graphene as an infinitesimally thin (two-sided) sheet with a local and isotropic conductivity, has been proposed. This model permits the derivation of analytical expressions for the electromagnetic field in the presence of a graphene sheet in terms of a dyadic Green function (represented using Sommerfeld integrals) and exciting electric current.[[330]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-330)

Even though these analytical models and methods can provide results for several canonical problems for benchmarking purposes, many practical problems involving graphene, such as the design of arbitrarily shaped electromagnetic devices, are analytically intractable. With the recent advances in the field of [computational electromagnetics (CEM)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_electromagnetics "Computational electromagnetics"), various accurate and efficient numerical methods have become available for analysis of electromagnetic field/wave interactions on graphene sheets and/or graphene-based devices. A comprehensive summary of computational tools developed for analyzing graphene-based devices/systems is proposed.[[331]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-331)

Graphene analogs[[332]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-332) (also referred to as "artificial graphene") are two-dimensional systems which exhibit similar properties to graphene. Graphene analogs have been studied intensively since the discovery of graphene in 2004. People try to develop systems in which the physics is easier to observe and manipulate than in graphene. In those systems, electrons are not always the particles that are used. They might be optical photons,[[333]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-333) microwave photons,[[334]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-334) plasmons,[[335]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-335) microcavity polaritons,[[336]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-336) or even atoms.[[337]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-337) Also, the honeycomb structure in which those particles evolve can be of a different nature than carbon atoms in graphene. It can be, respectively, a [photonic crystal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photonic_crystal "Photonic crystal"), an array of metallic rods, metallic [nanoparticles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoparticle "Nanoparticle"), a lattice of coupled microcavities, or an [optical lattice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_lattice "Optical lattice").

Graphene is a transparent and flexible conductor that holds great promise for various material/device applications, including solar cells,[[338]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-338) light-emitting diodes (LED), integrated photonic circuit devices,[[339]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-339)[[340]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-340) touch panels, and smart windows or phones.[[341]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-341) Smartphone products with graphene touch screens are already on the market.[[342]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-342)

In 2013, Head announced their new range of graphene tennis racquets.[[343]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-343)

As of 2015, there is one product available for commercial use: a graphene-infused printer powder.[[344]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-344) Many other uses for graphene have been proposed or are under development, in areas including electronics, [biological engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_engineering "Biological engineering"), [filtration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Filtration "Filtration"), lightweight/strong [composite materials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composite_materials "Composite materials"), [photovoltaics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photovoltaic "Photovoltaic") and [energy storage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy_storage "Energy storage").[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-auto-239)[[345]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-345) Graphene is often produced as a powder and as a dispersion in a polymer matrix. This dispersion is supposedly suitable for advanced composites,[[346]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-346)[[347]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-347) paints and coatings, lubricants, oils and functional fluids, capacitors and batteries, thermal management applications, display materials and packaging, solar cells, inks and 3D-printer materials, and barriers and films.[[348]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-348)

On 2 August 2016, [Briggs Automative Company's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Briggs_Automotive_Company "Briggs Automotive Company") new Mono model is said to be made out of graphene as the first of both a street-legal track car and a production car.[[349]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-349)

In January 2018, graphene-based spiral [inductors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inductor "Inductor") exploiting [kinetic inductance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinetic_inductance "Kinetic inductance") at room temperature were first demonstrated at the [University of California, Santa Barbara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Barbara "University of California, Santa Barbara"), led by [Kaustav Banerjee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaustav_Banerjee "Kaustav Banerjee"). These inductors were predicted to allow significant miniaturization in [radio-frequency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_frequency "Radio frequency")[integrated circuit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrated_circuit "Integrated circuit") applications.[[350]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-350)[[351]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-351)[[352]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-352)

The potential of epitaxial graphene on [SiC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SiC "SiC") for [metrology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metrology "Metrology") has been shown since 2010, displaying quantum Hall resistance quantization accuracy of three parts per billion in monolayer epitaxial graphene. Over the years precisions of parts-per-trillion in the Hall resistance quantization and giant quantum Hall plateaus have been demonstrated. Developments in the encapsulation and doping of epitaxial graphene have led to the commercialization of epitaxial graphene quantum resistance standards.[[353]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-353)

Novel uses for graphene continue to be researched and explored. One such use is in combination with water-based epoxy resins to produce anticorrosive coatings.[[354]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-354) The van der Waals nature of graphene and other two-dimensional (2D) materials also permits van der Waals heterostructures[[355]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-355) and integrated circuits based on [Van der Waals integration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Van_der_Waals_integration "Van der Waals integration") of 2D materials.[[356]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-356)[[357]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-357)

Graphene is utilized in detecting gasses and chemicals in environmental monitoring, developing highly sensitive biosensors for medical diagnostics, and creating flexible, wearable sensors for health monitoring.[[358]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-358)[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-359) Graphene's transparency also enhances optical sensors, making them more effective in imaging and spectroscopy.[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-360)

One review on graphene toxicity published in 2016 by Lalwani et al. summarizes the [in vitro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/In_vitro "In vitro"), [in vivo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/In_vivo "In vivo"), antimicrobial and environmental effects and highlights the various mechanisms of graphene toxicity.[[361]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-lalwani16-361) Another review published in 2016 by Ou et al. focused on graphene-family nanomaterials (GFNs) and revealed several typical mechanisms such as physical destruction, [oxidative stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidative_stress "Oxidative stress"), [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") damage, inflammatory response, [apoptosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apoptosis "Apoptosis"), [autophagy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autophagy "Autophagy"), and [necrosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necrosis "Necrosis").[[362]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-ou16-362)

A 2020 study showed that the toxicity of graphene is dependent on several factors such as shape, size, purity, post-production processing steps, oxidative state, functional groups, dispersion state, synthesis methods, route and dose of administration, and exposure times.[[363]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-363)

In 2014, research at [Stony Brook University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stony_Brook_University "Stony Brook University") showed that [graphene nanoribbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbon "Graphene nanoribbon"), graphene nanoplatelets, and graphene nano–onions are non-toxic at concentrations up to 50 μg/ml. These nanoparticles do not alter the differentiation of human bone marrow [stem cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stem_cell "Stem cell") towards [osteoblasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osteoblast "Osteoblast") (bone) or [adipocytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adipocyte "Adipocyte") (fat), suggesting that at low doses, graphene nanoparticles are safe for biomedical applications.[[364]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-364) In 2013, research at [Brown University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_University "Brown University") found that 10 μm few-layered graphene flakes can pierce [cell membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_membrane "Cell membrane") in solution. They were observed to enter initially via sharp and jagged points, allowing graphene to be internalized in the cell. The physiological effects of this remain unknown, and this remains a relatively unexplored field.[[365]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-brownedu-365)[[366]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphene#cite_note-li13-366)

*   [Borophene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Borophene "Borophene")– Allotrope of boron
*   [Carbon fiber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fiber "Carbon fiber")– Light, strong and rigid composite material
*   [Penta-graphene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Penta-graphene "Penta-graphene")
*   [Phagraphene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phagraphene "Phagraphene")
*   [Plumbene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plumbene "Plumbene")– Material made up of a single layer of lead atoms
*   [Silicene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicene "Silicene")– Two-dimensional allotrope of silicon
